The Objection to Possibility as a Real Attribute
Objector argues possibility is a rational judgment with three proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objector's argument that possibility is a rational judgment, not a real attribute, and the three proofs supporting this.

Core Idea
Possibility is a mental judgment, not a real attribute needing a substrate.

The objector counters the fourth proof by arguing that possibility (imkān) is simply a rational judgment, not a real quality of things. Whatever the mind can conceive without contradiction is called possible; whatever contradicts is impossible; whatever cannot be imagined as nonexistent is necessary. To support this, the objector presents three proofs that show possibility does not require an existent substrate. This passage is crucial because it challenges the very foundation of the philosophers' proof of the world's pre-eternity.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection statement
Read the opening lines where the objector defines possibility as a rational judgment.
Notice the three categories: possible, impossible, and necessary are all mind-dependent.
2
Study the three proofs
Examine each proof carefully, noting how each undermines the need for a substrate.
The first proof uses impossibility, the second uses blackness/whiteness, the third uses human souls.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this objection turns the philosophers' own views against them.
If souls are immaterial yet have possibility, then possibility does not require matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاعتراض أن يقال : الإمكان الذي ذكرتموه يرجع إلى قضاء العقل ، فكل ما قدر العقل وجوده فلم يمتنع عليه تقديره ، سميناه ممكناً و إن امتنع سميناه مستحيلا ، وإن لم يقد، على تقدير عدمه ، سميناه واجباً ، فهذه قضايا عقلية لا تحتاج إلى موجود حتى تجعل وصفاً له بدليل ثلاثة أمور . أحدها: أن الإمكان لو استدعى شيئاً موجوداً يضاف إليه ويقال : إنه إمكانه ، لاستدعى الامتناع شيئاً موجوداً يقال إنه امتناعه ، وليس للمتنع في ذاته وجود ، ولا مادة يطرأ عليها المحال حتى يضاف الامتناع إلى المادة . والثانى : أن السواد والبياض يقضى العقل فيهما قبل وجودهما بكونهما ممكنين، فإن كان هذا الإمكان مضافاً إلى الجسم الذي يطرآن عليه حتى يقال : معناه أن هذا الجسم يمكن أن يسود وأن يبيض ، فإذن ليس البياض في نفسه ممكناً ولا له نعت الإمكان ، وإنما الممكن الجسم والإمكان مضاف إليه ، فنقول : ما حكم نفس السواد في ذاته ، أهو ممكن ، أو واجب ، أو ممتنع ؟ ولابد من القول بأنه ممكن . فدل أن العقل فى القضية بالإمكان ، لا يفتقر إلى وضع ذات موجودة يضيف إليها الإمكان . والثالث : أن نفوس الآدميين عندهم جواهر قائمة بأنفسها ، ليست بجسم ولا مادة ، ولا منطبعة فى مادة ، وهى حادثة على ما اختاره ابن سينا والمحققون منهم ، ولها إمكان قبل حدوثها ، وليس لها ذات ولا مادة ، فإمكانها وصف إضافى ، ولا يرجع إلى قدرة القادر ، ولا إلى علمه بكونه ممكناً فإلى ماذا يرجع ؟ فينقلب عليهم هذا الإشكال.
The objection: It is said: The possibility you mentioned refers to the judgment of the intellect. For everything that the intellect estimates its existence and does not find impossible in its estimation, we call it possible. If it finds it impossible, we call it impossible. If it cannot estimate its non-existence, we call it necessary. These are rational judgments that do not require an existent to be described by them, as evidenced by three things. First: If possibility required something existent to which it is attributed and it is said to be its possibility, then impossibility would similarly require something existent to which it is attributed and it is said to be its impossibility. But the impossible in itself has no existence, nor is there matter upon which the impossible occurs so that impossibility could be attributed to matter. Second: The intellect judges blackness and whiteness before their existence as possible. If this possibility is attributed to the body in which they occur, and it is said that its meaning is that this body can become black or white, then blackness itself is not possible and does not have the attribute of possibility; rather, the body is possible and possibility is attributed to it. So we say: What is the judgment of blackness itself in its essence? Is it possible, necessary, or impossible? It must be said that it is possible. Thus the intellect in judging possibility does not require the positing of an existent essence to which possibility is attributed. Third: Human souls, according to them, are self-subsistent substances, not bodies, not matter, and not impressed in matter. They are originated, according to Ibn Sina and the investigators among them. They have possibility before their occurrence, yet they have no essence or matter. So their possibility is a relative attribute, and it does not refer to the power of the powerful, nor to His knowledge of their being possible. To what then does it refer? This objection turns back upon them.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage presents the objector's response to the fourth proof. The objector argues that possibility (imkān) is not a real attribute requiring a substrate; rather, it is merely a judgment of the intellect (qāḍā' al-'aql). Whatever the intellect can conceive as existing without contradiction is called possible; whatever is contradictory is impossible; whatever cannot be conceived as nonexistent is necessary. These are rational categories, not real properties. The objector provides three proofs. First, if possibility required an existent substrate, then impossibility would likewise require one, but the impossible has no existence and no matter, so impossibility cannot be attributed to anything. Second, consider blackness and whiteness: before they exist, the intellect judges them possible. If that possibility is attributed to the body, then blackness itself is not possible; yet we still ask whether blackness in itself is possible, and we must say it is possible. Thus the intellect does not need a real substrate. Third, human souls, according to Ibn Sina, are incorporeal substances without matter, yet they are originated and have possibility before existing. Since they have no matter, their possibility cannot be attributed to a substrate, nor can it be reduced to divine power or knowledge. Therefore the philosophers' own view implies that possibility does not need matter, and their proof is turned against them.

Key Terms

قضاء العقل
امتناع
مادة
نفس
جواهر قائمة بأنفسها

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Possibility is a real attribute inhering in a substrate.
Correction: The objector demonstrates that possibility is merely a rational judgment, as shown by the impossibility-proof (first proof), the blackness/whiteness-proof (second), and the soul-proof (third).

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the three reasons given by the objector to show that possibility is a rational judgment?
A) Impossibility does not have an existent substrate, so possibility should not either.
B) Blackness and whiteness are judged possible in themselves, not just in relation to a body.
C) Human souls are material and thus require matter for their possibility.
D) Human souls are immaterial yet have possibility, so matter is not necessary.

Key Takeaway
Possibility is a rational judgment of the intellect, not a real attribute that requires a substrate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often plan your life based on what you consider 'possible'—your career, relationships, health. But this lesson says possibility is merely a judgment of your intellect. Are you willing to admit that your estimation of what is possible might be an illusion, and that true possibility rests solely with Allah's will? Will you today, before you plan your next step, pause and say: 'I do not know if this is possible; only Allah knows' and submit your plans to Him?
?
The philosophers argued that even the souls before existence have possibility without any substrate. Consider your own soul: before you were born, did it have any attribute of possibility? This lesson says possibility is a mental judgment, not a real thing. How does this understanding affect your sense of self-importance? Are you living as if your soul had inherent possibilities you must realize, or do you recognize that your very existence is a gift that could have been impossible? Let this humble you and make you grateful.
?
The objection shows that impossibility does not require a substrate because the impossible does not exist. Think of sins or actions you consider impossible for you to commit—are you using that judgment as a false security? Recognize that impossibility is just a rational category, and only Allah knows your true capacity. Does this make you fear your own potential for evil and seek protection from Allah rather than relying on your intellect's limitations?

5 min
Blackness Depends on a Substrate
Ghazali refutes the claim that blackness is possible in itself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that blackness cannot be possible in itself and how it depends on a substrate for its existence.

Core Idea
Blackness has no independent existence; its possibility depends entirely on a body prepared for change.

Ghazali continues his critique of the philosophers by examining the claim that blackness is possible in itself. He argues that this is a mistake because any accident like blackness requires a substrate to exist. Without a body, blackness is impossible, not possible. Let's read his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the literal translation to get a feel for Ghazali's argument.
2
Identify the two scenarios
Notice that Ghazali considers two cases: blackness taken in isolation, and blackness as a state in a body. See how he judges the possibility in each.
The key is the substrate (majall).
3
Reflect on the implication
Understand that possibility belongs to the body's capacity for change, not to the accident itself. This refutes the idea that blackness has its own essence that could be possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثانى ، وهو كون السواد فى نفسه ممكناً ، فغلط ، فإنه إن خذ مجرداً دون مجل يحله ، كان ممتنعاً لا ممكناً ، وإنما يصير ممكناً إذا قدر هيأة في الجسم مهيأ لتبدل هيأة ، والتبدل ممكن على الجسم ، و إلا فليس للسواد نفس مفردة ، حتى توصف بإمكان
As for the second point, namely that blackness in itself is possible, it is a mistake. For if it is taken in isolation without a substrate to inhere in, it would be impossible, not possible. It only becomes possible if it is considered as a state in a body prepared for the change of state, and change is possible for the body. Otherwise, blackness has no single essence such that it can be described with possibility.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that blackness (al-sawad) is possible in itself. Ghazali argues that this is a mistake because blackness cannot exist independently; it must inhere in a substrate (a body). If taken in isolation without a substrate, it would be impossible, not possible. Blackness only becomes possible when considered as a state (hay'a) in a body that is prepared for a change of state. The change itself is possible for the body, but blackness has no separate essence (nafs mufrada) to which possibility can be attributed. Thus possibility is not intrinsic to blackness but belongs to the body's capacity for change.

Correction: Ghazali shows that blackness has no single essence (nafs mufrada) and only becomes possible when considered as a state in a body prepared for change.

Ghazali argues that blackness has no single essence that can be described as possible. The Possibility of the Soul Is Relational
Ghazali evaluates three positions on the soul's possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Ghazali demonstrates the soul's possibility is always relative to matter, regardless of whether the soul is eternal or created, impressed or not.

Core Idea
The soul's possibility is never intrinsic, always relative to matter.

Ghazali examines several philosophical positions on the soul's origin and nature. Strikingly, he finds a common thread: whether the soul is eternal or temporally originated, whether it is impressed in matter or merely related as a governor, its possibility is never an independent property but always depends on matter. This passage shows how he traces possibility back to matter in every case.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the continuous Arabic text once without stopping. Then read the English translation. Let the structure of different views emerge naturally.
2
Identify the three views on the soul
The passage presents three distinct positions: (1) soul eternal but attachable, (2) soul created and impressed in matter (Galen), (3) soul created but not impressed, yet related as governor. Find each one.
They are introduced by 'وأما الثالث' (the third point) and then contrasted.
3
See how each view ties possibility to matter
For each view, notice how Ghazali links the soul's possibility to matter. In the first, possibility attaches via attachment. In the second, via impression. In the third, via governance. The key is that in all cases, possibility is not intrinsic to the soul itself.
The phrase 'فالإمكان راجع إليها' appears at the end, summarizing the conclusion.
4
Understand Ghazali's conclusion
Ghazali does not argue for one view over another. Instead, he shows that regardless of which view one holds, the soul's possibility is always relational to matter. This undermines any claim that the soul has intrinsic possibility.
This is a dialectical move in the Tahafut: showing that the opponent's own views lead to a different conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثالث ، وهو النفس فهي قديمة عند فريق ، ولكن ممكن لها التعلق بالأبدان ، فلم يلزم على هذا ما قلتم . ومن سلم حدوثه ، فقد اعتقد فريق منهم منطبع في المادة تابع للمزاج على ما دل عليه كلام « جالينوس » فى بعض المواضع ، فتكون فى مادة ، وإمكانها مضاف إلى مادتها ، وعلى مذهب من سلم أنها حادثة وليست منطبعة ، فمعناه أن المادة ممكن لها أن يدبرها نفس ناطقة ، فيكون الإمكان السابق على الحدوث مضافاً إلى المادة، فإنها وإن لم تنطبع فيها فلها علاقة معها ، إذ هى المدبرة المستعملة لها، فيكون الإمكان راجعاً إليها بهذا الطريق
As for the third point—namely the soul—it is eternal according to one group, but it is possible for it to be attached to bodies, so what you said does not follow. For those who affirm its temporal origination, one group believes it is impressed in matter, following the temperament, as indicated by Galen's words in some places; thus it is in matter, and its possibility is relative to its matter. According to the doctrine of those who affirm its temporal origination and that it is not impressed, the meaning is that matter has the possibility of being governed by an intellective soul; thus the possibility prior to origination is relative to the matter. For even if it is not impressed in matter, it has a relationship with it, since it is the governor and user of it; so possibility reverts to matter in this way.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage discusses the possibility of the soul (al-nafs). Ghazali presents different philosophical views. First, some hold the soul is eternal but can be attached to bodies; in that case, its possibility is not intrinsic but relative to attachment. Second, among those who hold the soul is temporally originated, some (like Galen) believe it is impressed in matter and follows the temperament; thus its possibility is relative to its matter. Third, another group holds the soul is originated but not impressed; in this view, matter has the possibility of being governed by an intellective soul, so the possibility of the soul's existence is again relative to matter because of the soul's relationship as governor and user of the body. In all cases, the possibility of the soul is not an intrinsic attribute of the soul itself but is relational to matter.

Key Terms
النفس
الإمكان
المادة
منطبع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The soul has intrinsic possibility independent of matter.
Correction: Ghazali shows that whether eternal or created, impressed or not, the soul's possibility always depends on matter—either through attachment, impression, or governance.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the common factor in the different views on the soul's possibility?
A) The soul is eternal.
B) The soul is impossible.
C) The soul's possibility is relative to matter.
D) The soul is necessary.

Key Takeaway
The possibility of the soul is never an independent quality; it is always relational to matter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your soul's very possibility is not something you own—it is given through its relation to your body and its governance. How does that reshape your understanding of your utter dependence on Allah in every moment? What does that demand from you today?

If the soul's possibility is always relative to matter, then your existence as a soul is tied to your body and its actions. Will you live today as if your soul's governance of your body truly matters for eternity? What one change can you make right now?

Possibility Attributed to the Agent's Power
Ghazali refutes the substrate excuse and redefines possibility as agent-relative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's refutation of the philosophers' claim that possibility requires a material substrate and his alternative that possibility is a relation to the agent's power.

Core Idea
Possibility can be attributed to the agent's power, not just a substrate.

In this passage, Ghazali challenges the philosophers' argument that blackness and whiteness cannot be possible because they have no independent essence. He first shows that the intellect still conceives of them as possible essences. Then he introduces created souls as a stronger counterexample: they have possibility before existing without any substrate. Finally, he proposes that possibility is simply the relation of the agent's power to bring about the thing, regardless of imprinting.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the flow of refutation and proposal.
2
Identify the two main arguments
Find where Ghazali refutes the excuse about black and white, and where he gives his alternative attribution of possibility.
3
Understand the soul example
Why does Ghazali use originated souls as a counterexample? They have possibility before existing but no substrate.
4
See the agent-relative possibility
Ghazali says possibility is that the powerful agent can bring it about—a relation to the agent, not a substrate.
5
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this argument challenges the philosophers' framework: possibility is relational, not inherent in matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما العذر عن السواد والبياض بأنه لا نفس له ولا ذات منفردة، إن عنى بذلك في الوجود فنعم، وإن عنى بذلك في العقل فلا، فإن العقل يعقل السواد الكلى، ويحكم عليه بالإمكان في ذاته. ثم إن العذر باطل بالنفوس الحادثة، فإن لها ذواتاً مفردة وإمكاناً سابقاً على الحدوث، وليس ثمت ما يضاف إليه. وقولهم: إن المادة ممكن لها أن تدبرها النفوس، فهذه إضافات بعيدة، فإن اكتفيتم بهذا فلا يبعد أن يقال: معنى إمكان الحادث أن القادر عليه يمكن في حقه أن يحدثه، فتكون إضافة إلى الفاعل مع أنه ليس منطبعاً فيه، كما أنه إضافة إلى البدن المنفعل مع أنه لا تنطبع فيه، ولا فرق بين النسبة إلى الفاعل والنسبة إلى المنفعل إذا لم يكن انطباع في الموضعين.
As for the excuse regarding blackness and whiteness—that they have no self nor independent essence—if this is meant with respect to existence, then yes; but if it is meant with respect to the intellect, then no. For the intellect understands universal blackness and judges that it is possible in its essence. Moreover, the excuse is refuted by the case of originated souls, for they have independent essences and possibility prior to their origination, yet there is nothing to which it is attributed. And their saying: 'Matter is such that souls can manage it'—these are far-fetched attributions. If you are satisfied with this, then it is not far-fetched to say: The meaning of the possibility of an originated thing is that the powerful agent can bring it about, so it is an attribution to the agent, even though it is not imprinted in him, just as it is an attribution to the passive body even though it is not imprinted in it. And there is no difference between the relation to the agent and the relation to the patient when there is no imprinting in either case.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage tackles the philosophers' claim that blackness and whiteness have no independent essence, so their possibility cannot be attached to anything. Ghazali counters: in the mind, universal blackness is an essence and is judged possible. Then he introduces the example of created souls: they have independent essences and possibility before they exist, with no material substrate. If the philosophers try to attribute possibility to matter through the soul's relation, Ghazali dismisses it as far-fetched. He offers an alternative: possibility can be attributed to the agent's power (qudra) to bring about the thing, without any requirement of imprinting. This is a key argument that possibility is a relational concept, not a property of a substrate.

نفس (nafs)
ذات (dhat)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Possibility must inhere in a material substrate.
Correction: Possibility can be a relational concept, attributed to the agent's power to bring about the thing.

Checkpoint

B) Souls have possibility before existence without a substrate.
C) Souls are not essences.

Possibility is a relation to the agent's power, not a property of a substrate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Philosophers' Three Proofs for Future Eternity
Three arguments for future eternity presented.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three key arguments that philosophers use to claim the world will never end, based on causality, the nature of non-existence, and continuous possibility.

Core Idea
Philosophers argue the world's future eternity follows from its past eternity and the cause-effect relationship.

In this part of the Tahafut, Ghazali presents the philosophers' reasoning for the world's everlasting future. He notes that this issue is a direct extension of the previous one on past eternity. The same four proofs apply, but he focuses on three specific arguments. This lesson covers those arguments and their logical structure.

Guided Path
1
First proof: Cause and effect
The philosophers argue that since the cause (God) is eternal, the effect (world) must be eternal in both directions: no beginning and no end.
This argument assumes a necessary relationship between cause and effect.
2
Second proof: Non-existence after existence implies time
If the world ceased, its non-existence would have an 'after', which would require time to exist before that non-existence. Thus the world must be everlasting.
3
Third proof: Possibility is continuous
Since the possibility of existence is always present, the philosopher says existence itself can be continuous.
Ghazali will later critique this argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة [٢] في إبطال قولهم في أبدية العالم والزمان والمكان. ليعلم أن هذه المسألة فرع الأولى، فإن العالم عندهم كما أنه أزلي لا بداية لوجوده، فهو أبدي لا نهاية لآخره، ولا يتصور فساده ولا فناؤه، بل لم يزل كذلك، ولا يزال أيضاً كذلك. وأدلتهم الأربعة التي ذكرناها في الأزلية جارية في الأبدية، والاعتراض كالاعتراض من غير فرق. فإنهم يقولون: إن العالم معلول، وعلته أزلية أبدية فكان المعلول مع العلة، ويقولون: إذا لم تتغير العلة لم يتغير المعلول، وعليه بنوا منع الحدوث، وهو بعينه جار في الانقطاع. وهذا مسلكهم الأول. ومسلكهم الثاني: أن العالم إذا عدم فيكون عدمه بعد وجوده، فيكون له (بعد) ففيه إثبات الزمان. ومسلكهم الثالث: أن إمكان الوجود لا ينقطع، فكذلك الوجود الممكن يجوز أن يكون على وفق الإمكان، إلا أن هذا الدليل لا يقوى، فإنا نحيل أن يكون أزلياً، ولا نحيل أن يكون أبدياً، لو أبقاه الله تعالى أبداً، إذ ليس من ضرورة الحادث أن يكون له آخر، ومن ضرورة الفعل أن يكون حادثاً، وأن يكون له أول. ولم يوجب أن يكون للعالم لا محالة آخر إلا أبو الهذيل العلاف، فإنه قال: كما يستحيل في الماضي دورات لا نهاية لها، فكذلك في المستقبل؛ وهو فاسد، لأن كل المستقبل قط لا يدخل في الوجود لا متلاحقاً ولا متساوقاً، والماضي قد دخل كله في الوجود متلاحقاً، وإن لم يكن متساوقاً. وإذ قد تبين أنا لا نحيل بقاء العالم أبداً من حيث العقل، بل نجوز إبقاءه وإفناءه، فإنما يعرف الواقع من قسمي الممكن بالشرع، فلا يتعلق النظر فيه بالعقول.
Issue [2] on refuting their claim about the everlastingness (abadiyya) of the world, time, and space. Know that this issue is a branch of the first, for the world in their view, just as it is pre-eternal (azalī) with no beginning to its existence, so it is everlasting (abadī) with no end to its final term, and its corruption (fasād) and annihilation (fanā') are inconceivable. Rather, it has always been so, and it will always be so. The four proofs that we mentioned concerning pre-eternity (azaliyya) apply to everlastingness (abadiyya), and the objection is the same objection with no difference. For they say: The world is an effect (maʿlūl), and its cause is pre-eternal and everlasting, so the effect coexists with the cause. And they say: If the cause does not change, the effect does not change, and upon this they based the prevention of origination (ḥudūth), and the same applies to cessation (inqiṭāʿ). This is their first way. Their second way: That if the world were to cease to exist, its non-existence would be after its existence, so it would have an 'after' (baʿd), and in that is the affirmation of time. Their third way: That the possibility (imkān) of existence is not cut off, so likewise possible existence is permissible to be in accordance with possibility. However, this proof is not strong, for we deem it impossible for it to be pre-eternal (azalī), but we do not deem it impossible for it to be everlasting (abadī) if God Most High preserved it forever, for it is not necessary for an originated thing (ḥādith) to have an end, and it is necessary for an act (fiʿl) to be originated and to have a beginning. And no one made it necessary that the world must have an end except Abu al-Hudhayl al-Allaf, for he said: Just as it is impossible for past cycles without end, so likewise in the future. This is invalid, because the entire future is not entered into existence at all, neither successively nor synchronously, while the past has all entered into existence successively, even if not synchronously. Since it is clear that we do not deem it impossible for the world to remain forever from the standpoint of reason, but rather we permit its preservation and its annihilation, then what is actual from the two divisions of the possible is known only by the Law (sharʿ), and rational inquiry does not pertain to it.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage introduces Issue 2, which is the philosophers' claim that the world is everlasting in the future (abadiyya). Ghazali notes it is a branch of the first issue on past eternity (azaliyya). The same four proofs used for past eternity apply here. He enumerates three of their arguments: (1) The cause-effect relationship: since the cause is eternal, the effect is eternal. (2) If the world ceased, its non-existence would be after existence, implying time. (3) The possibility of existence is continuous, so existence should be continuous. Ghazali weakens the third argument by distinguishing between past and future eternity: we deem past eternity impossible (because no beginning), but we do not deem future eternity impossible if God preserves it forever, because an originated thing does not necessarily have an end. He refutes Abu al-Hudhayl's claim that infinite future cycles are impossible, arguing that the future never fully enters existence, unlike the past. He concludes that reason does not forbid future eternity; the actual outcome is known only from revelation.

Key Terms
أبدية
أزلية
معلول
حدوث
إمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the first argument for future eternity according to the philosophers?
A) The possibility of existence is continuous.
B) The cause-effect relationship requires the effect to be eternal like the cause.
C) Non-existence after existence implies time.
D) The world is not an originated thing.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers use three main arguments to support the world's future eternity: cause-effect, non-existence implying time, and continuous possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The third proof claims that what is possible must exist. When you plan your life, do you rely more on what seems possible or on Allah's will? Examine one area where you've trusted possibility over divine decree—how can you shift your trust?

Ghazali's Refutation and the Role of Revelation
Ghazali's refutation of the third proof and final conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's critique of the third argument for future eternity, his refutation of Abu al-Hudhayl, and his conclusion that future eternity is rationally possible but known only through revelation.

Core Idea
Reason allows the world to persist forever; revelation decides whether it will.

Ghazali now weakens the third proof and refutes an extreme view that denies infinite future cycles. He distinguishes between past and future eternity, arguing that reason does not forbid the world's endless preservation by God. The actual outcome, he insists, is known only from the Law (shar').

Guided Path
1
Ghazali's critique of the third proof
Ghazali argues that while past eternity is impossible (because it implies no beginning), future eternity is not impossible if God preserves the world.
He distinguishes between hadith (originated) and act: an act must have a beginning, but an originated thing does not necessarily have an end.
2
Refutation of Abu al-Hudhayl
Abu al-Hudhayl claimed infinite future cycles are as impossible as infinite past cycles. Ghazali rejects this because the future never fully enters existence.
The past has entered existence successively; the future is never actualized all at once.
3
Conclusion: Reason allows, revelation informs
Ghazali concludes that reason permits both preservation and annihilation. The actual state is known from the divine law, not rational speculation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة [٢] في إبطال قولهم في أبدية العالم والزمان والمكان. ليعلم أن هذه المسألة فرع الأولى، فإن العالم عندهم كما أنه أزلي لا بداية لوجوده، فهو أبدي لا نهاية لآخره، ولا يتصور فساده ولا فناؤه، بل لم يزل كذلك، ولا يزال أيضاً كذلك. وأدلتهم الأربعة التي ذكرناها في الأزلية جارية في الأبدية، والاعتراض كالاعتراض من غير فرق. فإنهم يقولون: إن العالم معلول، وعلته أزلية أبدية فكان المعلول مع العلة، ويقولون: إذا لم تتغير العلة لم يتغير المعلول، وعليه بنوا منع الحدوث، وهو بعينه جار في الانقطاع. وهذا مسلكهم الأول. ومسلكهم الثاني: أن العالم إذا عدم فيكون عدمه بعد وجوده، فيكون له (بعد) ففيه إثبات الزمان. ومسلكهم الثالث: أن إمكان الوجود لا ينقطع، فكذلك الوجود الممكن يجوز أن يكون على وفق الإمكان، إلا أن هذا الدليل لا يقوى، فإنا نحيل أن يكون أزلياً، ولا نحيل أن يكون أبدياً، لو أبقاه الله تعالى أبداً، إذ ليس من ضرورة الحادث أن يكون له آخر، ومن ضرورة الفعل أن يكون حادثاً، وأن يكون له أول. ولم يوجب أن يكون للعالم لا محالة آخر إلا أبو الهذيل العلاف، فإنه قال: كما يستحيل في الماضي دورات لا نهاية لها، فكذلك في المستقبل؛ وهو فاسد، لأن كل المستقبل قط لا يدخل في الوجود لا متلاحقاً ولا متساوقاً، والماضي قد دخل كله في الوجود متلاحقاً، وإن لم يكن متساوقاً. وإذ قد تبين أنا لا نحيل بقاء العالم أبداً من حيث العقل، بل نجوز إبقاءه وإفناءه، فإنما يعرف الواقع من قسمي الممكن بالشرع، فلا يتعلق النظر فيه بالعقول.
Issue [2] on refuting their claim about the everlastingness (abadiyya) of the world, time, and space. Know that this issue is a branch of the first, for the world in their view, just as it is pre-eternal (azalī) with no beginning to its existence, so it is everlasting (abadī) with no end to its final term, and its corruption (fasād) and annihilation (fanā') are inconceivable. Rather, it has always been so, and it will always be so. The four proofs that we mentioned concerning pre-eternity (azaliyya) apply to everlastingness (abadiyya), and the objection is the same objection with no difference. For they say: The world is an effect (maʿlūl), and its cause is pre-eternal and everlasting, so the effect coexists with the cause. And they say: If the cause does not change, the effect does not change, and upon this they based the prevention of origination (ḥudūth), and the same applies to cessation (inqiṭāʿ). This is their first way. Their second way: That if the world were to cease to exist, its non-existence would be after its existence, so it would have an 'after' (baʿd), and in that is the affirmation of time. Their third way: That the possibility (imkān) of existence is not cut off, so likewise possible existence is permissible to be in accordance with possibility. However, this proof is not strong, for we deem it impossible for it to be pre-eternal (azalī), but we do not deem it impossible for it to be everlasting (abadī) if God Most High preserved it forever, for it is not necessary for an originated thing (ḥādith) to have an end, and it is necessary for an act (fiʿl) to be originated and to have a beginning. And no one made it necessary that the world must have an end except Abu al-Hudhayl al-Allaf, for he said: Just as it is impossible for past cycles without end, so likewise in the future. This is invalid, because the entire future is not entered into existence at all, neither successively nor synchronously, while the past has all entered into existence successively, even if not synchronously. Since it is clear that we do not deem it impossible for the world to remain forever from the standpoint of reason, but rather we permit its preservation and its annihilation, then what is actual from the two divisions of the possible is known only by the Law (sharʿ), and rational inquiry does not pertain to it.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage introduces Issue 2, which is the philosophers' claim that the world is everlasting in the future (abadiyya). Ghazali notes it is a branch of the first issue on past eternity (azaliyya). The same four proofs used for past eternity apply here. He enumerates three of their arguments: (1) The cause-effect relationship: since the cause is eternal, the effect is eternal. (2) If the world ceased, its non-existence would be after existence, implying time. (3) The possibility of existence is continuous, so existence should be continuous. Ghazali weakens the third argument by distinguishing between past and future eternity: we deem past eternity impossible (because no beginning), but we do not deem future eternity impossible if God preserves it forever, because an originated thing does not necessarily have an end. He refutes Abu al-Hudhayl's claim that infinite future cycles are impossible, arguing that the future never fully enters existence, unlike the past. He concludes that reason does not forbid future eternity; the actual outcome is known only from revelation.

إفناء
أبدية
أزلية
حدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali denies that the world could be eternal in the future.
Correction: Ghazali actually affirms that future eternity is rationally possible if God preserves the world; he only denies past eternity.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why is Abu al-Hudhayl's argument invalid?
A) Future cycles are impossible.
B) The future never fully enters existence.
C) Past and future are the same in this respect.
D) Reason cannot decide the matter.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali argues that reason does not forbid the world's future eternity; the actual state is known only through revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face uncertainty about the future, do you trust reason alone or do you seek guidance from revelation? What does your current practice reveal about your reliance on the divine?
?
Ghazali says only the Law (sharʿ) can tell us what is actual in matters beyond reason. Are you content to let revelation be the final authority in your life, or do you sometimes prefer your own reasoning over what God has commanded?
?
How does the rational possibility of the world's persistence impact your conviction about the Day of Judgment? Is your belief based on rational speculation or on certain knowledge from the Book?

The First Two Arguments for the World's Perpetual Future
Ghazali states the issue and enumerates the first two proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali presents the philosophers' first two arguments for the world's perpetual future (the causal argument and the cessation argument) and his assertion that the same objections from pre-eternity apply.

Core Idea
The philosophers' arguments for a perpetual future mirror those for pre-eternity.

This lesson introduces Ghazali's engagement with the philosophers' claim that the world is eternally perpetual. He begins by stating that this issue is a branch of the pre-eternity issue and that the same four proofs and objections apply. We then see the first two proofs: the causal argument (the effect must coexist with its eternal cause) and the argument from cessation (which implies time). This sets the stage for understanding Ghazali's critical approach.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then the literal translation. Notice the concise style and the parallel structure with the pre-eternity discussion.
Pay attention to Ghazali's phrasing: 'والاعتراض كالاعتراض من غير فرق'
2
Identify the two proofs
Find where Ghazali introduces the first way (causal argument) and the second way (cessation argument). Understand the reasoning behind each.
The first proof hinges on the unchanging cause; the second on the implication of time from cessation.
3
Reflect on Ghazali's claim of identity
Note that Ghazali says the objections from pre-eternity apply identically here. Consider why this is significant for the overall refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة [٢] فى إبطال قولهم فى أبدية العالم والزمان والمكان ليعلم أن هذه المسألة فرع الأولى، فإن العالم عندهم كما أنه أزلى لا بداية لوجوده، فهو أبدى لا نهاية لآخره، ولا يتصور فساده ولا فناؤه، بل لم يزل كذلك، ولا يزال أيضاً كذلك وأدلتهم الأربعة التي ذكرناها في الأزلية جارية في الأبدية، والاعتراض كالاعتراض من غير فرق. فإنهم يقولون؛ إن العالم معلول، وعلته أزلية أبدية فكان المعلول مع العلة، ويقولون: إذا لم تتغير العلة لم يتغير المعلول، وعليه بنوا منع الحدوث، وهو بعينه جار في الانقطاع، وهذا مسلكهم الأول ومسلكهم الثانى: أن العالم إذا عدم فيكون عدمه بعد وجوده، فيكون له ( بعد ) ففيه إثبات الزمان . ومسلكهم الثالث: أن إمكان الوجود لا ينقطع، فكذلك الوجود الممكن يجوز أن يكون على وفق الإمكان، إلا أن هذا الدليل لا يقوى، فإنا نحيل أن يكون أزليا، ولا نحيل أن يكون أبديا، لو أبقاه الله تعالى أبداً، إذ ليس من ضرورة الحادث أن يكون له آخر، ومن ضرورة الفعل أن يكون حادثاً، وأن يكون له أول. ولم يوجب أن يكون للعالم لا محالة آخر إلا أبو الهذيل العلاف، فإنه قال: كما يستحيل فى الماضى دورات لا نهاية لها، فكذلك في المستقبل؛ وهو فاسد، لأن كل المستقبل قط لا يدخل فى الوجود لا متلاحقاً ولا متساوقاً والماضى قد دخل كله فى الوجود متلاحقاً، وإن لم يكن متساوقاً، وإذ قد تبين أنا لا نحيل بقاء العالم أبداً من حيث العقل، بل نجوز إبقاءه وإفناءه، فإنما يعرف الواقع من قسمى الممكن بالشرع، فلا يتعلق النظر فيه بالعقول
Issue [2]: On refuting their claim regarding the perpetual future of the world, time, and space. Let it be known that this issue is a branch of the first. For the world, according to them, just as it is pre-eternal with no beginning to its existence, so it is perpetually future with no end to its final term. Its corruption and annihilation are inconceivable; rather, it has always been so, and will always be so. And their four proofs which we mentioned concerning pre-eternity apply to perpetual futurity, and the objection is the same objection without any difference. For they say: the world is an effect, and its cause is pre-eternal and perpetually future, so the effect accompanies the cause. And they say: if the cause does not change, the effect does not change; and upon this they based the denial of origination (al-huduth), and it is exactly the same concerning cessation. This is their first way. Their second way: that if the world were to cease to exist, its non-existence would be after its existence, so it would have an 'after', and in this there is an affirmation of time. Their third way: that the possibility (imkan) of existence is not cut off, so likewise possible existence may be in accordance with possibility. Except that this proof is not strong, for we deem it impossible that it be pre-eternal, but we do not deem it impossible that it be perpetually future, if God Most High were to preserve it forever. For it is not a necessary property of an originated thing that it have an end, and it is a necessary property of an act that it be originated and that it have a beginning. And no one made it necessary that the world necessarily have an end except Abu al-Hudhayl al-Allaf, for he said: just as past cycles without end are impossible, so likewise in the future. And this is false, because all of the future never enters into existence, neither successively nor simultaneously, while the past has all entered into existence successively, though not simultaneously. And since it is clear that we do not deem the perpetual existence of the world impossible from the standpoint of reason, but rather we allow its preservation and its annihilation, then what actually occurs from the two divisions of the possible is known only by revelation (shar'), so rational consideration has no bearing on it.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage begins by stating the issue: refuting the philosophers' claim that the world is perpetually future (abadi), just as they claim it is pre-eternal (azali). Ghazali notes that this issue is a branch of the first issue (the pre-eternity of the world). He then says that the four proofs used for pre-eternity also apply to perpetual futurity, and the objections are the same. He lists the first three proofs. The first proof: the world is an effect, and its cause (God) is eternal in both directions, so the effect must be co-eternal with the cause both in pre-eternity and in perpetual futurity. The second proof: if the world were to cease, its non-existence would be after its existence, implying a temporal 'after', thus affirming time. The third proof: the possibility of existence never ceases, so the existence of the possible may continue forever. However, Ghazali criticizes this third proof: we do not consider it impossible for the world to be perpetually future if God preserves it, but we do consider it impossible for it to be pre-eternal because an originated thing must have a beginning. He then refutes Abu al-Hudhayl al-Allaf, who argued that just as infinite past cycles are impossible, so are infinite future cycles. Ghazali calls this false because the past has actually entered existence successively, whereas the future never does, so an infinite past is impossible but an infinite future is not. Finally, Ghazali concludes that reason does not forbid the perpetual future existence of the world; both preservation and annihilation are rationally possible. Which one actually occurs is known only through revelation, not reason.

أزلية
مسلك
علة
معلول
حدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The arguments for the world's pre-eternity do not apply to its perpetual futurity.
Correction: Ghazali explicitly states that the four proofs for pre-eternity apply to perpetual futurity without any difference, and the objections are the same.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the relationship between the arguments for pre-eternity and those for perpetual futurity?
A) They are entirely different.
B) They are the same without any difference.
C) The arguments for perpetual futurity are stronger.
D) Only the causal argument applies to both.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' case for a perpetual future world is based on the same reasoning as for pre-eternity, and Ghazali's refutation will mirror that earlier discussion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that the world will never end, how would that change the way you live your life today? What would you prioritize differently?
?
The philosophers argued that because God is eternal, His creation must also be eternal. Have you ever unconsciously assumed that God's attributes force certain limitations on His actions? How does this lesson challenge you to rethink God's absolute freedom?
?
This passage shows that even the brightest minds can build entire systems on flawed assumptions about God. In your own understanding of Allah, what is one assumption you hold that might not be as solid as you think? Will you examine it today?

The Third Argument: Possibility and Its Critique
Ghazali presents the third proof, critiques it, and refutes a specific philosopher.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's analysis of the philosophers' third argument from possibility for the world's perpetual future, his critique that pre-eternity is impossible but perpetual futurity is not, and his refutation of Abu al-Hudhayl's claim that infinite future is impossible.

Core Idea
Reason allows the world to be perpetually future; only revelation decides.

In this lesson, we examine the third proof offered by the philosophers for the world's perpetual future: the argument from the continuity of possibility. Ghazali critiques this proof, showing it is weak because while pre-eternity is impossible for an originated thing, perpetual futurity is not. He then refutes Abu al-Hudhayl al-Allaf who claimed that infinite future is as impossible as infinite past. Finally, Ghazali concludes that reason does not settle the matter; revelation does.

Guided Path
1
Read the third proof and its translation
Read the Arabic passage for the third proof carefully, then the literal translation. Notice the structure: statement of the proof, Ghazali's critique, refutation of Abu al-Hudhayl, and conclusion.
The proof is: 'إمكان الوجود لا ينقطع' – possibility does not cease.
2
Understand Ghazali's critique
Ghazali says the proof is weak because we do not deem it impossible for the world to be perpetual future if God preserves it, but we do deem it impossible to be pre-eternal. Understand the distinction he makes.
Key: 'نحيل أن يكون أزليا، ولا نحيل أن يكون أبديا'
3
Examine the refutation of Abu al-Hudhayl
Abu al-Hudhayl argued that infinite future cycles are impossible just as infinite past cycles are. Ghazali refutes this by noting that the past has already entered existence successively, while the future never does. This makes the two cases different.
Reflect on why this refutation is important for Ghazali's overall position.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Ghazali concludes that both preservation and annihilation are rationally possible. Which actually occurs is known only through revelation, not reason.
This limits the role of reason in eschatological matters.

Why does Ghazali consider the third proof weak?

?

The Fourth Argument: Matter and Possibility
Ghazali presents the fourth argument and concludes that only revelation decides the world's actual fate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth argument of the philosophers for the perpetual future of the world and why Ghazali rejects it.

Core Idea
Matter is permanent according to philosophers; Ghazali says this is impossible and only revelation decides.

In this passage, Ghazali completes his critique of the philosophers' four arguments for the world's perpetuity. He presents their fourth argument regarding the necessity of a permanent matter and then dismisses it as impossible. He also hints that there are additional proofs beyond these four, which he will address separately.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the literal translation. Notice the concise style as Ghazali summarizes the fourth argument.
2
Identify the philosophers' claims
Look for the key claims: possibility remains after non-existence, every originated thing requires prior matter, and matters/principles never become non-existent.
The terms 'māddah' (matter), 'aṣl' (principle), 'ṣūrah' (form), and 'ʻaraḍ' (accident) are important.
3
Understand Ghazali's response
Ghazali dismisses the argument as 'muhāl' (impossible) and refers to the previous answers for all such arguments. Note that he singles out this issue because the philosophers have two more proofs in it.
This indicates that Ghazali is not done with the debate; he will address further arguments later.
4
Reflect on the role of revelation
Consider why Ghazali emphasizes that only revelation can decide the world's actual fate, not rational arguments like this one.
This connects to the broader theme of the Tahāfut: the limits of reason in metaphysical matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما مسلكهم الرابع، فهو محال، لأنهم يقولون: إذا عدم العالم بقى إمكان وجوده، إذ الممكن لا ينقلب مستحيلاً، وهو وصف إضافي، فيفتقر كل حادث بزعمهم إلى مادة سابقة، وكل منعدم فيفتقر إلى مادة ينعدم عنها، فالمواد والأصول لا تنعدم، وإنما تنعدم الصور والأعراض الحالة فيها والجواب عن الكل ما سبق: وإنما أفردنا هذه المسألة لأن لهم فيها دليلين آخرين.
As for their fourth way, it is impossible (muhal). For they say: if the world becomes non-existent, the possibility of its existence remains, because the possible does not become impossible, and it is a relational attribute. So every originated thing, in their claim, requires a prior matter, and every non-existent thing requires a matter from which it becomes non-existent. Thus, matters and principles do not become non-existent; rather, only the forms and accidents subsisting in them become non-existent. And the answer to all is what preceded. And we only singled out this issue because they have two other proofs in it.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument of the philosophers for the perpetual future of the world. They argue that when the world ceases to exist, the possibility of its existence remains, because a possible thing does not become impossible. This possibility is a relational attribute. They claim that every originated thing requires a pre-existing matter, and every non-existent thing requires a matter from which it becomes non-existent. Therefore, matters (mawadd) and principles (usul) never become non-existent; only the forms and accidents that inhere in them cease to exist. Ghazali says this argument is impossible (muhal) and refers to the previous answer for all these arguments. He then notes that he singled out this issue because the philosophers have two additional proofs in it (beyond the four already discussed). This passage is brief and serves to complete the discussion of the four arguments and to mention the existence of further proofs.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The fourth argument is valid and proves the world's perpetual future.
Correction: Ghazali rejects it as impossible (muhāl) and reminds that only revelation can decide such matters.

Checkpoint
What is the philosophers' claim regarding matter (māddah) in the fourth argument?
A) That matter is created and will perish.
B) That matter and principles never become non-existent.
C) That matter is eternal and uncreated.
D) That forms and accidents are permanent.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' fourth argument relies on the permanence of matter, but Ghazali refutes it by referring to his earlier arguments and indicating that only revelation can determine the world's actual fate.

?

The claim that matter is permanent is a philosophical assumption. What 'matters' in your life—possessions, relationships, status—do you treat as if they will last forever? How can you use this reminder to turn your heart back to the Creator who alone is permanent?
?
When Ghazali dismisses the philosophers' argument as 'impossible,' he points to revelation as the final authority. Are there areas where you trust your own reasoning over divine guidance? Will you commit today to examine one such area and choose submission over logic?

Ghazali's Logical Critique of Galen's Solar Eternity Argument
Ghazali presents Galen's proof and two logical refutations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses logical analysis to challenge Galen's empirical argument for the sun's indestructibility, exposing hidden assumptions about corruption and observation.

Core Idea
Observation alone cannot prove necessity; logical assumptions must be scrutinized.

Galen, a renowned physician and philosopher, argued that the sun must be indestructible because it hasn't withered over millennia of observations. Ghazali responds not by denying the observations, but by dissecting the logical structure of the argument. He shows that the reasoning relies on unproven premises about the nature of corruption and the precision of human observation.

Guided Path
1
Read Galen's Argument
Carefully read the passage presenting Galen's claim: if the sun were corruptible, it would show withering over time; since no withering is observed, it is indestructible.
Notice the logical form: if P then Q; not Q, therefore not P.
2
Analyze the First Objection
Ghazali points out that the conditional 'if corrupt then wither' only holds if withering is the necessary or only mode of corruption. He argues that corruption could occur suddenly or through other means.
This challenges the necessity of the antecedent-consequent relationship.
3
Examine the Second Objection
Even if withering were the only mode, Ghazali questions whether we could detect it. Observations are approximate, and the sun's immense size means a mountain-sized loss would be imperceptible.
This questions the reliability of empirical evidence for such a claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول ما تمسك به جالينوس » إذ قال : لو كانت الشمس مثلا تقبل الانعدام ، لظهر فيها ذبول فى مدة مديدة ، والأرصاد الدالة على مقدارها منذ آلاف السنين لا تدل إلا على هذا المقدار ، فلما لم تذبل في هذه الآماد الطويلة ، دل على أنها لا تفسد الاعتراض عليه من وجوه . • الأول : أن شكل هذا الدليل أن يقال : إن كانت الشمس تفسد ، فلابد أن يلحقها ذبول ؛ لكن التالى محال ، فالمقدم محال ، وهذا قياس يسمى عندهم الشرطى المتصل » ، وهذه النتيجة غير لازمة ، لأن المقدم غير صحيح ، ما لم يضف إليه شرط آخر ، وهو قوله : إن كانت تفسد فلابد أن تذبل ، فهذا التالى لا يلزم هذا المقدم إلا بزيادة شرط وهو أن نقول : إن كانت تفسد فساداً ذبولياً فلابد أن تذبل في طول المدة ؛ أو يبين أنه لا فساد إلا بطريق الذبول حتى يلزم التالى للمقدم ، ولا نسلم له أنه لا يفسد الشيء إلا بالذبول بل الذبول ، أحد وجوه الفساد ، ولا يبعد أن يفسد الشيء بغتة وهو على حال كماله الثاني : أنه لو سلم هذا ، وأنه لا فساد إلا بالذبول ، فمن أين عرف أنه لا يعتريها الذبول ؟ ! أما التفاته إلى الأرصاد فمحال ، لأنها لا تعرف مقاديرها إلا بالتقريب ، والشمس التى يقال : إنها كالأرض مائة وسبعين مرة ، أو ما يقرب منه ، لو نقص منها مقدار جبال مثلا ، لكان لا يتبين للحس ، فلعلها ...
The first is what Galen adhered to, as he said: If the sun, for example, were susceptible to annihilation, deterioration would appear in it over a long period. And the observations indicating its size for thousands of years indicate only this size. Since it has not withered in these long ages, that indicates that it does not corrupt. The objection to it from several aspects. The first: The form of this proof is to say: If the sun corrupts, then deterioration must affect it. But the consequent is impossible, so the antecedent is impossible. This is a syllogism called among them 'conditional conjunctive'. But this conclusion is not necessary, because the antecedent is not correct unless another condition is added to it. It is his saying: If it corrupts, it must wither. This consequent does not follow from this antecedent except by adding a condition, which is that we say: If it corrupts with a withering corruption, then it must wither over a long period. Or he must show that there is no corruption except by way of withering so that the consequent follows from the antecedent. But we do not grant him that a thing only corrupts by withering; rather, withering is one of the modes of corruption. And it is not unlikely that a thing corrupts suddenly while it is in a state of perfection. The second: Even if this is granted, and that there is no corruption except by withering, how does he know that withering does not befall it? As for his reliance on observations, it is impossible, because their magnitudes are only known approximately. And the sun, which is said to be about 170 times the earth, or approximately, if an amount like mountains were lost from it, it would not be perceptible to the senses. Perhaps it...
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage presents Galen's argument for the indestructibility of the sun based on observational constancy, followed by two objections. Galen argued that if the sun were subject to annihilation, it would show signs of withering over a long time; since observations over millennia show no change in size, it does not corrupt. The first objection: Ghazali analyzes the logical form as a conditional conjunctive syllogism (sharṭī muttaṣil): if the sun corrupts, then it withers; the consequent (withering) is false (observed not), so the antecedent (corruption) is false. Ghazali points out that this conclusion only holds if withering is the necessary and only mode of corruption. But we do not concede that; withering is just one mode, and corruption could occur suddenly. The second objection: Even if we accept that corruption only occurs via withering, how can we be sure that the sun has not withered? Observations are approximate; the sun's immense size means a loss of a mountain-sized amount would be imperceptible. So the argument fails.

Key Terms
ذبول (deterioration/withering)
أرصاد (observations)
شرطى متصل (conditional conjunctive syllogism)
مقدم (antecedent)
تالي (consequent)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Empirical observation alone can definitively prove the indestructibility of a celestial body.
Correction: Ghazali shows that logical assumptions (e.g., modes of corruption) and the limits of human perception prevent empirical evidence from being conclusive.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali's first objection, why does Galen's conditional syllogism not prove the sun is indestructible?
A) The observations are false.
B) The consequent (withering) does not necessarily follow from the antecedent (corruption) unless it is the only mode.
C) The sun has actually withered but we can't see it.
D) The syllogism is invalid because it is conditional.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali demonstrates that empirical arguments for metaphysical claims often hide logical assumptions that must be critically examined.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the world around you – the sun, the mountains, your own body – how often do you assume that because something has remained unchanged for a long time, it always will? Where in your life today could that assumption be blinding you to the reality of change and decay that Allah has decreed?
?
Ghazali shows that hidden assumptions can make an argument seem solid when it is not. Think of a belief you hold firmly – perhaps about your own security, your health, or your ability to control your life. What unexamined condition might you be adding that makes your conclusion seem necessary? Are you willing to question it?
?
If the sun, a massive and constant sign of Allah's power, could be annihilated in an instant without warning, what does that teach you about your own reliance on anything other than Allah? What 'sun' in your life do you treat as if it cannot be taken away?

The Second Proof Against the World's Non-Existence
Ghazali presents the philosophers' second proof that the world cannot cease to exist.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' second proof for the impossibility of the world's non-existence and Ghazali's analysis of its logical structure.

Core Idea
The philosophers' second proof argues that non-existence would require either a change in God or an impossible eternal will.

In this passage, Ghazali presents the second philosophical argument against the possibility of the world ceasing to exist. The proof attempts to show that no cause can be conceived that would annihilate the world without implying a change in God or a contradiction in the nature of an eternal will. This argument is a classic example of kalam reasoning about divine attributes and temporal events.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Let the argument structure emerge before analyzing.
Notice the conditional phrasing: 'إذا...' and 'فلا يخلو...'
2
Identify the proof's logical structure
The proof is a conditional: if the world were to cease, there must be a cause. That cause leads to two impossible alternatives. Identify each part.
3
Examine the two alternatives
The cause could be God's will (but that would require change in God) or it could be something else (but then an eternal will would produce a temporal effect, which was already ruled impossible).
The second alternative relies on earlier arguments about eternal will.
4
Reflect on the overall conclusion
The philosophers conclude that since both alternatives are impossible, the world must be eternal in its existence and cannot be annihilated.
Ghazali is presenting this as a claim to be refuted later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدليل الثاني لهم في استحالة عدم العالم أن قالوا : إن العالم لا تنعدم جواهره ، لأنه لا يعقل سبب معدم لها ، وما لم يكن منعدماً ثم انعدم ، فلابد أن يكون بسبب ، وذلك السبب لا يخلو ، إما أن يكون إرادة القديم سبحانه وهو محال ، لأنه إذا لم يكن مريداً لعدمه ، ثم صار مريداً ، فقد تغير ، أو يؤدى إلى أن يكون القديم وإرادته على نعت واحد في جميع الأحوال ، والمراد يتغير من العدم إلى الوجود ثم من الوجود إلى العدم. وما ذكرناه من استحالة وجود حادث بإرادة قديمة ، يدل على استحالة العدم
The second proof for the impossibility of the non-existence of the world: They said: The atoms (jawahir) of the world do not cease to exist, because no cause that would annihilate them is conceivable. And what was not non-existent then became non-existent must be by a cause. That cause is either the will of the Eternal, exalted be He—which is impossible, because if He was not willing its non-existence and then became willing, He would have changed—or it leads to the Eternal and His will being in one state in all circumstances while the intended object changes from non-existence to existence and then from existence to non-existence. And what we mentioned about the impossibility of a temporal event arising from an eternal will indicates the impossibility of non-existence.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof by the philosophers for the impossibility of the world's non-existence. It begins by stating that the atoms (jawahir) of the world cannot cease to exist because no cause to annihilate them is conceivable. If they were to cease, a cause would be needed. The cause could be either God's will or something else. If God's will changes from not willing non-existence to willing it, that implies change in God, which is impossible. Alternatively, if God's will is eternal and the intended object changes, then the same problem arises as before: an eternal will cannot produce a temporal effect. Thus, non-existence is impossible.

Key Terms
جواهر
عدم
إرادة قديمة
تغير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think this proof assumes the world is eternal and therefore cannot be destroyed.
Correction: The proof does not assume eternity; rather, it argues that if the world were to cease, a cause would be needed, and any such cause leads to an impossibility, thus concluding the world cannot cease.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, why is the non-existence of the world impossible?
A) Because God's will cannot change.
B) Because the cause of non-existence would require an infinite regress.
C) Because the atoms of the world are indestructible by nature.
D) Because any cause for non-existence leads to change in God or an impossible relation.

Key Takeaway
This proof, while refuted by Ghazali, highlights the importance of the coherence between divine will and temporal events.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the philosophers are wrong and the world could cease to exist at any moment, are you living with the urgency of that reality? Does your heart truly depend on God's will for every breath you take?
?
The philosophers argued that an eternal will cannot produce temporal changes—but you believe God creates each moment anew. Does your daily life reflect this belief, or do you behave as if the world is self-sufficient and permanent?
?
If you were asked to defend the possibility of the world's annihilation using Islamic teachings, would you know how to respond? Are you prepared to stand firm on the truth that God alone sustains and may end existence?

The Stronger Problem: Non-Existence Cannot Be an Act
Ghazali presents a stronger problem: non-existence cannot be an act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why non-existence cannot be considered an act of God, based on the premise that an act must produce something existent.

Core Idea
Non-existence is nothing, so it cannot be the result of an act.

Ghazali deepens his critique of the philosophers by presenting a more fundamental problem. He argues that even if we accept that God's will is eternal, the concept of 'annihilating' the world as an act faces a logical difficulty: non-existence is not a thing that can be brought about. In this passage, he challenges us to identify what exactly God would be doing if He were to cause non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Pay attention to the question: 'so how can it be an act?'
2
Identify the core premise
Notice that Ghazali insists that an act must produce something existent. He states that the least degree of an act is that the agent brings about existence.
This premise is crucial for the dilemma.
3
Analyze the dilemma
Consider the two options Ghazali presents: either God's act is the existence of the world (impossible because existence has ceased) or it is the non-existence of the world (non-existence is not a thing). Understand why both fail.
This shows that non-existence cannot be an act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويزيد ههنا إشكال آخر أقوى من ذلك ، هو أن المراد فعل المريد لا محالة وكل من لم يكن فاعلاً ثم صار فاعلاً ، إن لم يتغير هو في نفسه ، فلابد أن يصير فعله موجوداً بعد أن لم يكن موجوداً ، فإنه لو بقى كما كان ، إذ لم يكن له فعل ، والآن أيضاً لا فعل له ، فإذن لم يفعل شيئاً ؛ والعدم ليس بشيء فكيف يكون فعلا ؟ ! فإذا أعدم العالم وتجدد له فعل لم يكن ، فها ذلك الفعل ؟ ! أهو وجود العالم ؟ ! وهو محال ، إذ انقطع الوجود ! أو فعله عدم العالم ؟! وعدم العالم ليس بشيء أن يكون موجوداً وعدم العالم ليس شيئاً موجوداً حتى يقال : هو الذي فعله حتى يكون فعلا ، فإن أقل درجات الفعل الفاعل ، وأوجده الموجد
And here there is another, stronger problem: namely, the intended object (al-murad) is necessarily the act of the agent. And everyone who was not an agent then became an agent, if he does not change in himself, then his act must come into existence after it was not. For if he remained as he was—since he had no act—and now also has no act, then he did nothing. And non-existence (al-'adam) is not a thing, so how can it be an act? So if He annihilates the world and a new act occurs for Him that did not exist, what is that act? Is it the existence of the world? That is impossible because existence has ceased. Or is His act the non-existence of the world? But the non-existence of the world is not a thing that is existent, and the non-existence of the world is not an existent thing such that it can be said: 'He did it' for it to be an act. For the least degree of the act is that the agent brings about existence.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage presents a stronger problem than the previous proof. It argues that the intended object (al-murad) is necessarily the act of the agent. If an agent was not acting and then becomes acting, his act must come into existence. If he does not change, then his act must be a new existent. But non-existence is not a thing, so it cannot be an act. If God were to annihilate the world, what act would He perform? It cannot be the existence of the world because that has ceased. It cannot be non-existence because non-existence is not an existent thing. The least degree of an act is that the agent brings about existence. Therefore, non-existence cannot be an act.

Key Terms
مراد
فعل
فاعل
عدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think God can simply 'will' non-existence as an act.
Correction: The argument shows that even willing requires an act that produces something real; non-existence is nothing, so it cannot be the result of an act.

Checkpoint
What is the central problem with saying that God's act is the non-existence of the world?
A) Non-existence is a thing that is created.
B) Non-existence is not an existent thing that can be the product of an act.
C) God cannot will non-existence because it would contradict His will to create.
D) The world cannot cease to exist because it is eternal.

Key Takeaway
Non-existence is nothing and therefore cannot be the result of an act; an act must produce something existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If non-existence is nothing and cannot be an act, then your very existence at this moment is a direct act of God. He is keeping you in existence right now. When you sin or neglect His commands, are you living as if your existence is a mere given rather than a continuous gift? What one thing will you do today to acknowledge that your being is sustained by His act?
?
Ghazali argues that annihilation is not a positive act—God does not 'do' non-existence. This means that your fear of death and loss is ultimately a fear of something that is not a real act of God. Does this truth free you from anxiety about the future? Or does it make you more aware that your entire existence depends on His will? How will you trust Him with your fears starting now?
?
If the least degree of an act is to bring about existence, then every moment you exist is a new act of God. Consider one blessing you take for granted (health, faith, a relationship). How would your relationship with God change if you saw that blessing as a fresh act of creation every moment? What step will you take today to respond with gratitude?

The Philosophers' Principle of Indestructible Substances
Al-Ghazali reports the philosophers' argument that annihilation of substances is impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that self-subsistent entities cannot be annihilated and how they analogize to the human soul.

Core Idea
Philosophers argue that self-subsistent substances are indestructible by nature.

Al-Ghazali now presents the philosophers' counter-argument. Having rejected theological theories of annihilation, the philosophers assert that self-subsistent entities—like the world and the human soul—cannot be annihilated. This passage states their core principle and its application.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own wording come through.
Notice that the passage begins with a conditional statement.
2
Identify the general principle
Find the sentence that states the philosophers' general rule about what cannot be annihilated. Note the key terms: 'qā'im bi-nafsihī' and 'lā fī maḥall'.
This principle is the core of their metaphysical view.
3
Understand the analogy
The passage mentions the human soul as an example of an originated substance that cannot be annihilated. Ask yourself: How does this analogy support the conclusion about the world?
The philosophers concede origination but deny annihilation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالت الفلاسفة : إذا بطلت هذه الطرق ، لم يبق وجه للقول بجواز إعدام العالم . ولو قيل بأن العالم حادث ، فإنهم مع تسليمهم حدوث النفس الإنسانية يدعون استحالة انعدامها بطريق يقرب مما ذكرناه وبالجملة : عندهم كل قائم بنفسه لا في محل ، لا يتصور انعدامه بعد
And the philosophers said: If these ways are invalid, there remains no ground for asserting the permissibility of the annihilation of the world. Even if it is said that the world is originated, they—while conceding the origination of the human soul—claim the impossibility of its annihilation in a way close to what we have mentioned. In summary: according to them, every self-subsistent entity not in a locus cannot be conceived to be annihilated after [existence].
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' conclusion. They argue that since all the (theological) theories of annihilation have been refuted, there is no basis for claiming the world can be annihilated. They analogize to the human soul: even if it is originated, it cannot be annihilated. Their general principle: any self-subsistent entity (qā'im bi-nafsihī) not inhering in a locus—i.e., a substance—cannot be annihilated after coming into existence. This reflects the philosophers' view that the world is eternal and its annihilation is impossible.

Key Terms
الفلاسفة (al-falāsifa)
قائم بنفسه (qā'im bi-nafsihī)
لا في محل (lā fī maḥall)
انعدام (in'idām)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers deny that the world or souls ever come into existence.
Correction: The philosophers accept the origination of the human soul; they only deny the possibility of annihilation after existence.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, what kind of entity cannot be annihilated?
A) Accidents
B) Composite bodies
C) Self-subsistent entities not in a locus
D) Things that have opposite accidents

Key Takeaway
The philosophers hold that any self-subsistent substance, once existing, cannot be annihilated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosophers claim that a self-subsistent entity like your soul cannot be annihilated. Do you rest in the security of that claim, or do you live in the awe of a God who holds the power to annihilate everything at any moment? How does your answer shape your daily reliance on Him?
?
Consider your attachment to your own existence. If you truly believed that God can annihilate you in an instant, would you live differently? Would your priorities shift? What concrete change can you make today to reflect that dependence?
?
The philosophers' principle exalts created substances by granting them indestructibility. Are there areas of your life where you attribute permanence or self-sufficiency to something other than God? What steps can you take to redirect that reverence back to the Creator?
