Ghazali restricts discussion to one division
Ghazali presents an objection about water turning to vapor and responds by focusing on one division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's strategic response to the philosophers' argument about material change and how he narrows the debate to one division.

Core Idea
Ghazali focuses on one case rather than refuting all material change arguments.

In this passage, Ghazali presents the philosophers' objection that water does not perish but merely changes form via a permanent underlying matter. Rather than rebut each of their divisions in detail, he announces he will restrict the discussion to a single division, setting up a more targeted critique. This is a key strategic move that shapes the subsequent argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection from the philosophers
Read carefully how they explain water turning to vapor as a change of form, not annihilation.
Notice the key terms: hayula (matter), sura (form).
2
Identify Ghazali's response
Ghazali says he could defend each division but chooses to focus on one. Identify that strategic decision.
3
Reflect on the strategy
Ask yourself why Ghazali would limit the discussion. What advantage does this give him?
This focuses the debate on a single, more manageable case.
4
Understand the implication
Recognize that Ghazali is not rejecting the philosophers' entire theory but isolating a key case for scrutiny.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مهما أوقدت النار تحت الماء انعدم الماء ، قالوا لم ينعدم ، ولكن انقلب بخاراً ثم ماء ، فالمادة وهى الهيولى باقية في الهواء، وهي المادة التي كانت محلاً لصورة الماء ، وإنما خلعت الهيولى صورة المائية ، ولبست صورة الهوائية ، و إذا أصاب الهواء برد ، تكثف وانقلب ماء ، لا أن مادة تجددت ، بل المادة مشتركة بين العناصر ، وإنما تتبدل عليها صورها . الجواب : أن ما ذكرتموه من الأقسام وإن أمكن أن نذب عن كل واحد، ونبين أن إبطاله على أصلكم لا يستقيم ، لاشتمال أصولكم على ما هو من جنسه ولكنا لا نطول به ونقتصر على قسم واحد.
When it is said to them: Whenever fire is lit under water, the water perishes, they say it does not perish but turns into vapor then water. The matter (matter) which is the hylic substrate remains in the air, and it is the matter that was a locus for the form of water; the hylic substrate has merely shed the form of wateriness and put on the form of airiness. And when the air is struck by cold, it condenses and turns into water, not that a new matter has come into being; rather, the matter is common among the elements, and only their forms change upon it. The answer: As for the divisions you have mentioned, even if we could defend each one and show that its refutation is not sound according to your principles, because your principles include what is of the same kind, we will not prolong (the discussion) and will restrict ourselves to one division.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage begins with an objection from the opponents. They argue that when fire heats water, water does not actually cease to exist; rather, it transforms into vapor and then back into water. They posit a permanent underlying matter (the hylic substrate, al-hayula) that persists through changes of form. The wateriness form is replaced by an airiness form, and later when cooled, it becomes water again. The implication is that nothing is truly created or annihilated; only forms change on a common substrate. Ghazali responds by stating that although he could refute each of their divisions (i.e., each of the various ways they explain change without real creation/destruction), he will not do so at length. Instead, he will restrict himself to a single division, indicating a strategic focus. This sets the stage for the more detailed argument that follows. The core idea is that the opponent's material account of change is not the issue; Ghazali will directly address whether nonexistence can be an effect of a cause.

صورة
مائية
هوائية
انقلاب

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the opponent's material change explanation disproves creation ex nihilo
Correction: But Ghazali does not address that here; he focuses on a different division.

What does Ghazali do after presenting the opponent's division of cases?

B) Restricts himself to one division

D) Changes the subject

Key Takeaway
Ghazali strategically limits the debate to one division to keep the argument focused and avoid unnecessary length.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter doubts about your faith that come in many forms, do you try to answer every single objection, or do you focus on the one root issue that matters most? What does Ghazali's approach teach you about where to direct your energy?
?
Think of a time when you felt overwhelmed by the complexity of a religious question. How could the principle of 'restricting to one division' help you find clarity and avoid being distracted by secondary issues?
?
Right now, what is the one area of your belief or practice that you have been avoiding because it seems too complicated? Will you commit to focusing on that one division, as Ghazali did, until you have answered it clearly for yourself?

intermediate

Nonexistence as an Effect of Divine Power
Ghazali defends nonexistence as an effect of divine power, answering objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that nonexistence can be an effect of divine power, and how he answers the objection that nonexistence is not a thing.

Core Idea
Nonexistence can be an effect because its occurrence is intelligible, even if it is not a thing.

This passage is from Ghazali's Tahafut al-Falasifa, where he defends the Ash'ari occasionalist view that both existence and nonexistence are directly caused by God's will. The philosophers objected that nonexistence cannot be an effect because it is not a thing. Here, Ghazali turns their logic against them, arguing that if nonexistence is not a thing, then it cannot occur at all—yet we clearly observe things ceasing to exist. Therefore, its occurrence is intelligible, and it can be attributed to divine power. Let's unpack his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall argument. Notice the structure: thesis, objections, answers, and a parallel.
2
Identify Ghazali's thesis
Ghazali asserts that bringing into existence and bringing into nonexistence are both acts of the Powerful by will. The agent does not change; the action changes.
The key is that 'action' here includes nonexistence as a new occurrence.
3
Follow the objection-and-answer structure
There are two objections: (1) What act is produced from God when He annihilates? (2) How can nonexistence be produced if it is not a thing? Trace how Ghazali answers each.
4
Reflect on the parallel
Ghazali draws a comparison with someone who denies that nonexistence can supervene on accidents. Why does he use this parallel?
It shows that the opponent's own reasoning leads to absurdity.
5
Summarize the key move
Ghazali argues that if the occurrence of nonexistence is intelligible, then attributing it to power is also intelligible, regardless of whether we call it a 'thing'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بم تنكرون على من يقول : الإيجاد والإعدام بإرادة القادر ، فإذا أراد الله سبحانه وتعالى أوجد ، وإذا أراد أعدم ، وهذا معنى كونه سبحانه قادراً على الكمال ، وهو في جملة ذلك لا يتغير في نفسه ، وإنما يتغير الفعل ، وأما قولكم : إن الفاعل لابد أن يصدر منه فعل ، فما الصادر منه ؟ ! قلنا الصادر منه ما تجدد ، وهو العدم ، إذ لم يكن عدم ، ثم تجدد العدم ، فهو الصادر عنه. فإن قلتم : إنه ليس بشيء ، فكيف صدر عنه ؟ ! قلنا : وهو ليس بشيء ، فكيف وقع ؟ ! وليس معنى صدوره عنه ، إلا أن وقع ، مضاف إلى قدرته ، فإذا عقل وقوعه ، لم لا تعقل إضافته إلى القدرة ؟ ! وما الفرق بينكم وبين من ينكر طريان العدم أصلا على الأعراض والصور ، ويقول : العدم ليس بشيء ، فكيف يطرأ ، وكيف يوصف بالطريان والتجدد ؟ ! ولا نشك فى أن العدم يتصور طريانه على الأعراض والصور ، فالموصوف بالطريان معقول وقوعه ، سمى شيئاً أو لم يسم ، فإضافة ذلك الواقع المعقول إلى قدرة القادر أيضاً معقول.
What do you say to one who says: Bringing into existence and bringing into nonexistence occur by the will of the Powerful (al-Qadir). When Allah, exalted, wills, He brings into existence, and when He wills He annihilates. This is the meaning of His being perfectly powerful. In all of that, He does not change in Himself; rather, the action changes. As for your statement: The agent must produce an act; what is the act produced from Him? We say: What is produced from Him is what has newly come to be, and that is nonexistence, since there was no nonexistence, then nonexistence came to be new, so it is what is produced from Him. If you say: It is not a thing (laysa bi-shay'), how then is it produced from Him? We say: And it is not a thing, so how does it occur? The meaning of its being produced from Him is only that it occurs, added to His power. If its occurrence is intelligible, why is its attribution to power not intelligible? What difference is there between you and one who denies that nonexistence can supervene upon accidents and forms at all, and says: Nonexistence is not a thing, so how can it supervene, and how can it be described as supervening or becoming new? We do not doubt that nonexistence can be conceived to supervene upon accidents and forms. So that which is described as supervening, its occurrence is intelligible, whether it is called a thing or not. So the attribution of that intelligible occurrence to the power of the Powerful is also intelligible.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is a central argument in Ghazali's defense of occasionalism. He begins by presenting the doctrine of the Ash'ari: that creation and annihilation are both directly caused by God's will. The agent does not change in His essence; only the act changes. The opponents object that an agent must produce an act; what act is produced from God when He annihilates? Ghazali's answer is that the produced thing is nonexistence itself, which newly occurs (yatajaddad). The opponents then counter that nonexistence is not a thing, so how can it be produced? Ghazali turns the objection around: if nonexistence is not a thing, how can it occur at all? The very occurrence of nonexistence is intelligible; we can conceive of something ceasing to exist. Thus, if its occurrence is intelligible, its attribution to the agent's power is also intelligible. He then draws a parallel to those who deny that nonexistence can supervene upon accidents and forms at all, arguing that if nonexistence is not a thing, then it cannot be described as supervening. Yet we clearly understand that when a color changes, the previous color ceases to exist. So his point is that the intelligibility of the occurrence of nonexistence is sufficient for it to be an effect of the Creator. The core idea here is that Ghazali is defending the notion that nonexistence can be an effect without being a positive entity; it is a relational concept.

الإعدام
القادر
العدم
الصادر

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Nonexistence cannot be an effect because it is not a positive entity.
Correction: Ghazali argues that the intelligibility of the occurrence of nonexistence—its being conceived—is sufficient for it to be attributed to divine power.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the act produced from God when He annihilates?
A) A new substance
B) Nonexistence itself
C) A change in the accident
D) Nothing

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's key move is that the intelligibility of the occurrence of nonexistence is sufficient for it to be an effect of divine power, contrary to the philosophers' objection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if your very existence at this moment is sustained directly by God's power? If you are honest, do you sometimes assume that you simply 'continue to exist' on your own? What would change in your daily awareness if you truly believed that your nonexistence is as much in His hands as your existence?
?
When you lose something you love—a relationship, a possession, a capability—do you trace that loss back to God's will, or do you attribute it to mere chance or secondary causes? How would your heart respond if you saw the loss as an act of the Powerful (al-Qadir) as much as the gain?
?
The philosophers argued that nonexistence cannot be an act of God because it is not a thing. In what areas of your life do you resist seeing God's involvement in what is not—the absences, the failures, the endings? Are you willing to submit even those to His power and admit that He is the one who brings them about?

The Entailment Argument: Does Whiteness Entail Non-Existence of Blackness?
Ghazali's first logical argument against the claim that accidents only cease by opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's first argument that the supervention of an opposite entails the non-existence of the previous quality, and that this non-existence is an originated intelligible thing.

Core Idea
Non-existence can be an effect; it is not nothing, but an originated reality.

In this passage, Ghazali continues his refutation of the philosophers' claim that accidents cease only by the arrival of an opposite. He presents a logical dilemma to show that the supervention of whiteness must entail the non-existence of blackness, and that this non-existence must be originated and intelligible, thus subject to divine power.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the structure: a question, objection, then two arguments. We focus on the first argument.
The first argument is a series of questions to force a logical conclusion.
2
Identify the main question
Ghazali asks: Does the supervention of whiteness entail the non-existence of blackness? The opponent must answer yes or no.
3
Trace the logical implications
If yes, then the entailer is either the same as the entailed or different. Neither option works without admitting that non-existence is an originated intelligible thing.
If the entailer is different, then it is either eternal or originated.
4
Conclude the argument
Since non-existence is originated and intelligible, it can be attributed to God's power. This undermines the opponent's denial of annihilation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: هذا إنما يلزم على مذهب من يجوز عدم الشيء بعد وجوده، فيقال له: ما الذي طرأ؟ ! وعندنا لا ينعدم الشيء الموجود، وإنما معنى انعدام الأعراض طريان أضدادها، التى هي موجودات، لا طريان العدم المجرد الذي ليس بشيء، فإن ما ليس بشيء كيف يوصف بالطريان؟ ! فإذا أبيض الشعر فالطارئ هو البياض فقط وهو موجود، ولا نقول: الطارئ عدم السواد. وهذا فاسد من وجهين: أحدهما: أن طريان البياض هل تضمن عدم السواد أم لا؟ ! فإن قالوا: لا، فقد كابروا العقول، وإن قالوا: نعم، فالمتضمن غير المتضمن أم عينه؟ ! فإن قالوا هو عينه كان متناقضاً، إذ الشيء لا يتضمن نفسه، وإن قالوا: غيره، فذلك الغير معقول أم لا؟ ! فإن قالوا: لا، قلنا: فيم عرفتم أنه متضمن؟ ! والحكم عليه بكونه متضمناً اعتراف بكونه معقولا، وإن قالوا: نعم فذلك المتضمن المعقول وهو عدم السواد قديم أو حادث؟ ! فإن قالوا: قديم، فهو محال، وإن قالوا: حادث، فالموصوف بالحدوث كيف لا يكون معقولا؟ ! و إن قالوا: لا قديم ولا حادث فهو محال، لأنه قبل طريان البياض، لو قيل السواد معدوم كان كذباً، وبعده إذا قيل: إنه معدوم كان صدقاً، فهو طارئ لا محالة، فهذا الطارئ معقول، فيجوز أن يكون منسوباً إلى قدرة قادر. الوجه الثاني: أن من الأعراض ما ينعدم عندهم لا بضده، فإن الحركة لا ضد لها، وإنما التقابل بينها وبين السكون عندهم تقابل الملكة والعدم، أعنى تقابل الوجود والعدم، ومعنى السكون عدم الحركة، فإذا عدمت الحركة لم يطرأ سكون هو ضده، بل هو عدم محض، وكذلك الصفات التي هي من قبيل الاستكمال، كانطباع أشباح المحسوسات فى الرطوبة الجليدية من العين، بل انطباع صور المعقولات فى النفس، فإنها ترجع إلى استفتاح وجود من غير زوال.
For it may be said: This (the previous argument) applies only according to the doctrine of one who holds that a thing can become nonexistent after its existence. For it is said to him: What has supervened? However, according to us, an existing thing does not become nonexistent; rather, the meaning of the annihilation of accidents is the supervention of their opposites, which are existents, not the supervention of bare nonexistence which is not a thing. For that which is not a thing, how can it be described as supervening? So when the hair becomes white, the supervening thing is whiteness alone, and it is existent; we do not say: the supervening thing is the nonexistence of blackness. This is invalid from two aspects: First: Does the supervention of whiteness entail the nonexistence of blackness or not? If they say no, they are contradicting reason. If they say yes, then is the entailer the same as the entailed or other than it? If they say it is the same, that is contradictory, for a thing does not entail itself. If they say it is other, then is that other thing intelligible or not? If they say no, we ask: How did you know that it entails it? The judgment that it entails (tadammun) is an admission that it is intelligible. If they say yes, then that intelligible entailer which is the nonexistence of blackness—is it eternal or originated? If they say eternal, it is impossible. If they say originated, then how is that which is described as originated not intelligible? And if they say neither eternal nor originated, that is impossible, because before the supervention of whiteness, if one said 'blackness is nonexistent' it would be false, and after it, if one said it is nonexistent, it would be true. So it is supervening without doubt. Thus this supervening thing is intelligible, and it is permissible that it be attributed to the power of a Powerful One. Second: Among accidents there are some that, according to them, become nonexistent not by an opposite. For motion has no opposite; the opposition between it and rest is, according to them, the opposition of possession (al-malaka) and privation, i.e., the opposition of existence and nonexistence. The meaning of rest is nonexistence of motion. So when motion ceases, there does not supervene a rest which is its opposite; rather, it is pure nonexistence. Likewise, attributes that are of the type of perfection (istikmal), such as the impression of forms of sensible objects in the vitreous humor of the eye, indeed, the impression of intelligible forms in the soul—they all go back to a beginning of existence without any removal (of anything).
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage continues the refutation of the opponents who argued that annihilation does not happen; rather, accidents only cease by the supervention of their opposites. Ghazali shows this view is invalid in two ways. The first argument: When whiteness supervenes on hair, does it entail the nonexistence of blackness? If the opponent says no, they deny obvious causality. If yes, then is the entailment the same as the entailed or different? It cannot be the same because a thing does not entail itself. So it must be different. That different thing (the nonexistence of blackness) must be either eternal or originated. It cannot be eternal because blackness existed before whiteness, so its nonexistence cannot be eternal. It must be originated. If it is originated, it is intelligible. Therefore, nonexistence can be an effect of a cause. The second argument: Some accidents, like motion, have no opposite. Motion ceases and rest is not opposite but defined as privation of motion. So in their own system, when motion ceases, there is pure nonexistence (the absence of motion). Similarly, for impressions in the eye or soul, the cessation of one impression is not due to an opposite but a new act of existence. This shows that the opponents cannot consistently deny that nonexistence can supervene; they implicitly accept it in many cases. The core idea is that the opponent's own examples (motion, impressions) force them to admit nonexistence as a supervening state.

Key Terms
الضد
الطريان
التضمن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that nonexistence is pure nothing and cannot be caused.
Correction: Ghazali shows that nonexistence can be an intelligible originated state that is entailed by the supervention of an opposite.

Checkpoint
What does Ghazali force the opponent to admit about the nonexistence of blackness after whiteness supervenes?
A) It is eternal
B) It is non-intelligible
C) It is originated and intelligible
D) It does not exist

Key Takeaway
Nonexistence can be an originated effect; the opponent's own logic forces them to admit that nonexistence supervenes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a change in the world—like a black cloth turning white—do you instinctively think that the blackness was destroyed by something, or do you assume the whiteness merely replaced it? How does Ghazali's argument challenge your default assumption about causation? Will you now consider that nonexistence itself might be a direct act of Allah, not merely a byproduct?
?
In your own life, you experience things ceasing: thoughts, feelings, opportunities. Do you tend to attribute their end to an opposite (e.g., a negative thought replacing a positive one) or do you recognize that Allah can simply bring nonexistence without an opposite? How would this shift in perspective affect your reliance on Allah for both existence and non-existence?
?
Ghazali forces the opponent to admit that nonexistence is 'intelligible' and 'originated'. Do you treat the concept of 'nothingness' as something real in your mind? When you pray for something to be removed from your life, do you trust that Allah can cause that thing to cease without needing a replacement? Commit to reflecting on one area of your life where you fear emptiness, and ask Allah to grant you the certainty that He is the Governor of both presence and absence.

The Second Argument: Accidents Without Opposites
Ghazali's second argument using accidents without opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's second argument that some accidents, like motion, cease without an opposite, proving that the opponent's definition of cessation is inconsistent.

Core Idea
Motion has no opposite; its cessation is pure nonexistence, not replacement by an opposite.

Ghazali presents a second, more direct refutation by pointing to accidents that, according to the philosophers themselves, have no opposites. Motion, rest, and impressions in the soul are examples where cessation cannot be explained by the arrival of an opposite. This shows that the opponent implicitly accepts pure annihilation in many cases.

Guided Path
1
Read the second argument
Read the Arabic and translation for the second argument. Notice the examples: motion, rest, and impressions in the eye and soul.
2
Understand the example of motion
Motion has no opposite. Rest is defined as the absence of motion (privation). So when motion ceases, rest is not an opposite but pure nonexistence.
3
Consider the impression example
Impressions in the eye or soul do not cease by an opposite; they begin by a new act of existence without removing a prior thing.
4
Conclude the inconsistency
The opponent's own system admits nonexistence as a supervening state in these cases, so they cannot deny it generally.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: هذا إنما يلزم على مذهب من يجوز عدم الشيء بعد وجوده، فيقال له: ما الذي طرأ؟ ! وعندنا لا ينعدم الشيء الموجود، وإنما معنى انعدام الأعراض طريان أضدادها، التى هي موجودات، لا طريان العدم المجرد الذي ليس بشيء، فإن ما ليس بشيء كيف يوصف بالطريان؟ ! فإذا أبيض الشعر فالطارئ هو البياض فقط وهو موجود، ولا نقول: الطارئ عدم السواد. وهذا فاسد من وجهين: أحدهما: أن طريان البياض هل تضمن عدم السواد أم لا؟ ! فإن قالوا: لا، فقد كابروا العقول، وإن قالوا: نعم، فالمتضمن غير المتضمن أم عينه؟ ! فإن قالوا هو عينه كان متناقضاً، إذ الشيء لا يتضمن نفسه، وإن قالوا: غيره، فذلك الغير معقول أم لا؟ ! فإن قالوا: لا، قلنا: فيم عرفتم أنه متضمن؟ ! والحكم عليه بكونه متضمناً اعتراف بكونه معقولا، وإن قالوا: نعم فذلك المتضمن المعقول وهو عدم السواد قديم أو حادث؟ ! فإن قالوا: قديم، فهو محال، وإن قالوا: حادث، فالموصوف بالحدوث كيف لا يكون معقولا؟ ! و إن قالوا: لا قديم ولا حادث فهو محال، لأنه قبل طريان البياض، لو قيل السواد معدوم كان كذباً، وبعده إذا قيل: إنه معدوم كان صدقاً، فهو طارئ لا محالة، فهذا الطارئ معقول، فيجوز أن يكون منسوباً إلى قدرة قادر. الوجه الثاني: أن من الأعراض ما ينعدم عندهم لا بضده، فإن الحركة لا ضد لها، وإنما التقابل بينها وبين السكون عندهم تقابل الملكة والعدم، أعنى تقابل الوجود والعدم، ومعنى السكون عدم الحركة، فإذا عدمت الحركة لم يطرأ سكون هو ضده، بل هو عدم محض، وكذلك الصفات التي هي من قبيل الاستكمال، كانطباع أشباح المحسوسات فى الرطوبة الجليدية من العين، بل انطباع صور المعقولات فى النفس، فإنها ترجع إلى استفتاح وجود من غير زوال.
For it may be said: This (the previous argument) applies only according to the doctrine of one who holds that a thing can become nonexistent after its existence. For it is said to him: What has supervened? However, according to us, an existing thing does not become nonexistent; rather, the meaning of the annihilation of accidents is the supervention of their opposites, which are existents, not the supervention of bare nonexistence which is not a thing. For that which is not a thing, how can it be described as supervening? So when the hair becomes white, the supervening thing is whiteness alone, and it is existent; we do not say: the supervening thing is the nonexistence of blackness. This is invalid from two aspects: First: Does the supervention of whiteness entail the nonexistence of blackness or not? If they say no, they are contradicting reason. If they say yes, then is the entailer the same as the entailed or other than it? If they say it is the same, that is contradictory, for a thing does not entail itself. If they say it is other, then is that other thing intelligible or not? If they say no, we ask: How did you know that it entails it? The judgment that it entails (tadammun) is an admission that it is intelligible. If they say yes, then that intelligible entailer which is the nonexistence of blackness—is it eternal or originated? If they say eternal, it is impossible. If they say originated, then how is that which is described as originated not intelligible? And if they say neither eternal nor originated, that is impossible, because before the supervention of whiteness, if one said 'blackness is nonexistent' it would be false, and after it, if one said it is nonexistent, it would be true. So it is supervening without doubt. Thus this supervening thing is intelligible, and it is permissible that it be attributed to the power of a Powerful One. Second: Among accidents there are some that, according to them, become nonexistent not by an opposite. For motion has no opposite; the opposition between it and rest is, according to them, the opposition of possession (al-malaka) and privation, i.e., the opposition of existence and nonexistence. The meaning of rest is nonexistence of motion. So when motion ceases, there does not supervene a rest which is its opposite; rather, it is pure nonexistence. Likewise, attributes that are of the type of perfection (istikmal), such as the impression of forms of sensible objects in the vitreous humor of the eye, indeed, the impression of intelligible forms in the soul—they all go back to a beginning of existence without any removal (of anything).
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage continues the refutation of the opponents who argued that annihilation does not happen; rather, accidents only cease by the supervention of their opposites. Ghazali shows this view is invalid in two ways. The first argument: When whiteness supervenes on hair, does it entail the nonexistence of blackness? If the opponent says no, they deny obvious causality. If yes, then is the entailment the same as the entailed or different? It cannot be the same because a thing does not entail itself. So it must be different. That different thing (the nonexistence of blackness) must be either eternal or originated. It cannot be eternal because blackness existed before whiteness, so its nonexistence cannot be eternal. It must be originated. If it is originated, it is intelligible. Therefore, nonexistence can be an effect of a cause. The second argument: Some accidents, like motion, have no opposite. Motion ceases and rest is not opposite but defined as privation of motion. So in their own system, when motion ceases, there is pure nonexistence (the absence of motion). Similarly, for impressions in the eye or soul, the cessation of one impression is not due to an opposite but a new act of existence. This shows that the opponents cannot consistently deny that nonexistence can supervene; they implicitly accept it in many cases. The core idea is that the opponent's own examples (motion, impressions) force them to admit nonexistence as a supervening state.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think all changes involve replacement of one quality by another.
Correction: Ghazali shows motion and impressions cease without replacement; their cessation is pure absence.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the relationship between motion and rest according to the philosophers?
A) Rest is an opposite of motion
B) Rest is the privation of motion
C) Motion and rest are both accidents that supervene
D) Motion has no opposite and rest does not exist

Key Takeaway
Some accidents like motion cease by pure nonexistence, proving that annihilation is real.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You've seen that motion can cease into pure nothingness. What attachments in your life are you holding onto as if they were permanent, when in fact they could vanish without replacement? Will you cling to them anyway?
?
This argument shows that Allah can annihilate without needing an opposite. How does this reality affect your fear of losing something or someone? Are you prepared to face the annihilation of all you love for His sake?
?
If every impression in your soul is a new beginning from nonexistence, what does that say about your thoughts and desires? Are you purifying your heart's impressions, knowing they are not forced out by opposites but can be simply removed by Allah's will?

Causality and Created Things
advanced
6 min
Ghazali's First Objection: Non-Existence as Intelligible Occurrent
Ghazali's first objection: forcing admission that non-existence is intelligible and caused.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that the philosophers' own reasoning forces them to admit non-existence as an intelligible occurrence that can be attributed to divine power.

Core Idea
If an opposite entails non-existence, then non-existence is intelligible and causeable.

Ghazali begins by stating the philosophers' claim that accidents perish only by the occurrence of existent opposites, not by pure non-existence. He then raises a dialectical challenge: if the occurrence of an opposite entails the non-existence of its predecessor, then that non-existence must be intelligible—and if intelligible, it can be caused by God. This first objection unpacks that logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' position
Read the initial lines where Ghazali states the philosophers' claim. Note their insistence that only existents occur, not pure non-existence.
2
Follow the dialectical chain
Work through Ghazali's series of questions: Does the opposite entail non-existence? Is the entailer the same as the entailed? Is the entailed intelligible? Follow each step to see the forced conclusion.
Notice the logical traps: if they deny entailment, they defy reason; if they affirm, they must admit non-existence as an intelligible occurrent.
3
Identify the conclusion
Ghazali concludes that non-existence is an intelligible occurrent that can be attributed to the power of a capable agent. This directly refutes the philosophers' initial denial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأعراض طريان أضدادها ، التى هى موجودات ، لا طريان العدم المجرد الذي ليس بشيء ، فإن ما ليس بشيء كيف يوصف بالطريان ؟ ! فإذا أبيض الشعر فالطارئ هو البياض فقط وهو موجود ، ولا نقول : الطارئ عدم السواد
وهذا فاسد من وجهين :
أحدهما : أن طريان البياض هل تضمن عدم السواد أم لا ؟ ! فإن قالوا : لا ، فقد كابروا العقول ، وإن قالوا : نعم ، وإن قالوا : نعم ، فالمتضمن غير المتضمن أم عينه ؟ ! فإن قالوا هو عينه كان متناقضاً، إذ الشيء لا يتضمن نفسه، وإن قالوا : غيره، فذلك الغير معقول أم لا ؟ ! فإن قالوا : لا ، قلنا : فيم عرفتم أنه متضمن ؟ ! والحكم عليه بكونه متضمناً اعتراف بكونه معقولا ، وإن قالوا : نعم المتضمن المعقول وهو عدم السواد قديم أو حادث ؟ ! فإن قالوا : قديم ، فهو محال ، وإن قالوا : حادث ، فالموصوف بالحدوث كيف لا يكون معقولا ؟ ! و إن قالوا : لا قديم ولا حادث فهو محال ، لأنه قبل طريان البياض ، لو قيل السواد معدوم كان كذباً ، وبعده إذا قيل : إنه معدوم كان صدقاً ، فهو طارئ لا محالة ، فهذا الطارئ معقول ، فيجوز أن يكون منسوباً إلى قدرة قادر
الوجه الثاني : أن من الأعراض ما ينعدم عندهم لا بضده ، فإن الحركة لا ضد لها ، وإنما التقابل بينها وبين السكون عندهم تقابل الملكة والعدم ، أعنى تقابل الوجود والعدم ، ومعنى السكون عدم الحركة ، فإذا عدمت الحركة لم يطرأ سكون هو ضده ، بل هو عدم محض ، وكذلك الصفات التي هي من قبيل الاستكمال ، كانطباع أشباح المحسوسات فى الرطوبة الجليدية من العين ، بل انطباع صور المعقولات فى النفس ، فإنها ترجع إلى استفتاح وجود من غير زوال ضده ، وإذا عدمت كان معناها زوال الوجود من غير استعقاب ضده ، فزوالها عبارة عن عدم محض قد طرأ ؛ فعقل وقوع العدم الطارئ ؛ وما عقل وقوعه بنفسه وإن لم يكن شيئاً . عقل أن ينسب إلى قدرة القادر فتبين بهذا أنه مهما تصور وقوع حادث بإرادة قديمة ، لم يفترق الحال بين أن يكون الواقع عدماً أو وجوداً . والله أعلم .
Accidents [perish by] the occurrence of their opposites, which are existents, not the occurrence of pure non-existence which is not a thing. For how can what is not a thing be described by occurrence? Thus when hair becomes white, the occurrent is whiteness only, and it is an existent. We do not say: the occurrent is the non-existence of blackness.
This is corrupt in two respects:
The first: Does the occurrence of whiteness entail the non-existence of blackness or not? If they say no, they have defied reason. If they say yes—and if they say yes—is the entailer other than the entailed or the same? If they say it is the same, that is contradictory, for a thing does not entail itself. If they say it is other, is that other intelligible or not? If they say no, we say: how did you know it entails? The judgment that it entails is an admission that it is intelligible. If they say yes, that intelligible entailer, which is the non-existence of blackness—is it eternal or originated? If they say eternal, that is impossible. If they say originated, how can that which is described by origination not be intelligible? If they say neither eternal nor originated, that is impossible, because before the occurrence of whiteness, if one said "blackness is non-existent," it would be false, and after it, if one said it is non-existent, it would be true. So it is an occurrence without doubt. So this occurrent is intelligible, and it is permissible that it be attributed to the power of an omnipotent.
The second aspect: Some accidents perish according to them not by their opposite. For motion has no opposite; the opposition between it and rest according to them is the opposition of disposition and privation, i.e., the opposition of existence and non-existence. The meaning of rest is the non-existence of motion. So when motion ceases, no rest that is its opposite occurs; rather it is pure non-existence. Similarly, attributes that are of the kind of perfecting, such as the impression of images of sensible objects in the vitreous humor of the eye, indeed the impression of intelligible forms in the soul—for they revert to the acquisition of existence without the removal of an opposite. When they cease, their meaning is the removal of existence without being followed by an opposite. So their removal is an expression of pure non-existence that has occurred. Thus the occurrence of non-existence is intellectually conceivable; and that which is intellectually conceivable in itself, even if it is not a thing, is conceivable to be attributed to the power of the Omnipotent. So it is made clear by this that whenever the occurrence of an event by an eternal will is conceived, the situation does not differ between what occurs being non-existence or existence. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' claim that accidents (a‘rāḍ) cease to exist only by the occurrence of their opposites, not by pure non-existence. Ghazali argues that non-existence ( ‘adam) itself is an intelligible occurrence that can be caused by divine power.

The passage begins by stating the philosophers' position: they hold that accidents perish by the occurrence of their opposites, which are existents, not by the occurrence of pure non-existence, because non-existence is not a thing and cannot be said to "occur". For example, when hair turns white, the occurrent is whiteness, not the non-existence of blackness.

Ghazali then declares that this view is corrupt in two respects.

The first objection (al-wajh al-awwal) focuses on the relation between the occurrence of an opposite and the non-existence of its predecessor. He asks: does the occurrence of whiteness entail the non-existence of blackness? If the philosophers deny entailment, they defy reason. If they affirm it, then we ask: is the entailer (the whiteness) the same as the entailed (the non-existence of blackness) or different? If they say the same, that is contradictory because a thing cannot entail itself. If they say different, is that different thing intelligible (ma‘qūl)? If they say no, then how can they claim it is entailed? Judgment of entailment implies intelligibility. If they say yes, then that intelligible thing—the non-existence of blackness—is it eternal (qadīm) or originated (ḥādith)? If they say eternal, that is impossible because non-existence is not eternal. If they say originated, then how can something described by origination not be intelligible? If they say neither, that is impossible because before the occurrence of whiteness, the statement "blackness is non-existent" is false, and after it, it is true. Therefore, non-existence undoubtedly occurs. Since this occurrent is intelligible, it can be attributed to the power of a capable agent (qādir).

The second objection (al-wajh al-thānī) points out that some accidents have no opposite. For example, motion (ḥaraka) has no opposite; the opposition between motion and rest (sukūn) is, according to the philosophers, the opposition of disposition and privation (malaka wa ‘adam), i.e., existence and non-existence. Rest is simply the non-existence of motion. When motion ceases, no positive opposite occurs; it is pure non-existence. Similarly, qualities of perfection like the impression of sensible images in the eye or intelligible forms in the soul involve acquisition of existence without removal of an opposite. When they cease, it is the removal of existence without being followed by an opposite. Thus, their removal is pure non-existence that has occurred. Since the occurrence of non-existence is intellectually conceivable, it can be attributed to divine power.

The conclusion: it becomes clear that whenever an event occurs by an eternal will, it makes no difference whether what occurs is non-existence or existence. God knows best.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Non-existence is not a real effect and cannot be caused by God.
Correction: Ghazali argues that non-existence is an intelligible occurrent that can be directly caused by divine power, as the dialectical reasoning shows.

Checkpoint
In Ghazali's first objection, after establishing that non-existence is intelligible, what question does he then ask about its nature?
A) Is it eternal or originated?
B) Is it a substance or an accident?
C) Does it have an opposite?
D) Can it be perceived by the senses?

Key Takeaway
Non-existence, though not a thing, is an intelligible occurrence that can be directly caused by God's power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If non-existence is directly caused by God's power, is there any area of your life where you feel that 'nothing' or absence is outside His control? How does this challenge your trust in His sovereignty?
?
When you experience loss—whether of a relationship, health, or opportunity—do you recognize that the very non-existence of what you lost is a deliberate act of God? How can this understanding deepen your patience and acceptance?
?
Ghazali forced the philosophers to admit that non-existence is an intelligible occurrence. In what ways do you treat 'nothing' as irrelevant to your faith? How can you begin to see every moment of emptiness as a creation of God that demands a response?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate

Ghazali's Second Objection: Accidents Without Opposites
Ghazali's second objection: examples of accidents that cease without opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's demonstration that some accidents perish by pure non-existence, without requiring an opposite, thereby proving that non-existence can be an effect of divine power.

Core Idea
Motion and perfections perish by pure non-existence, not by opposites.

Ghazali's second objection shifts from dialectical reasoning to empirical examples. He points out that many accidents, such as motion and qualities of perfection, have no inherent opposite. Their cessation is simply the removal of existence—pure non-existence. This shows that non-existence can occur and can be willed by God.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about motion
Read the beginning of the second objection. Ghazali states that motion has no opposite; its opposition with rest is that of existence and non-existence. Understand the philosophers' own distinction between 'disposition and privation'.
2
Examine the qualities of perfection
Ghazali gives examples like the impression of images in the eye and intelligible forms in the soul. These are acquired without removing an opposite. When they cease, it is pure non-existence.
3
Draw the conclusion
Since non-existence can occur (as in these cases), it is conceivable as an effect of divine power. Thus, the philosophers' claim that accidents only perish by opposites is false.

Accidents [perish by] the occurrence of their opposites, which are existents, not the occurrence of pure non-existence which is not a thing. For how can what is not a thing be described by occurrence? Thus when hair becomes white, the occurrent is whiteness only, and it is an existent. We do not say: the occurrent is the non-existence of blackness.

If they say originated, how can that which is described by origination not be intelligible? If they say neither eternal nor originated, that is impossible, because before the occurrence of whiteness, if one said "blackness is non-existent," it would be false, and after it, if one said it is non-existent, it would be true. Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' claim that accidents (a‘rāḍ) cease to exist only by the occurrence of their opposites, not by pure non-existence. Ghazali argues that non-existence ( ‘adam) itself is an intelligible occurrence that can be caused by divine power.

The passage begins by stating the philosophers' position: they hold that accidents perish by the occurrence of their opposites, which are existents, not by the occurrence of pure non-existence, because non-existence is not a thing and cannot be said to "occur". For example, when hair turns white, the occurrent is whiteness, not the non-existence of blackness.

Ghazali then declares that this view is corrupt in two respects.

The first objection (al-wajh al-awwal) focuses on the relation between the occurrence of an opposite and the non-existence of its predecessor. He asks: does the occurrence of whiteness entail the non-existence of blackness? If the philosophers deny entailment, they defy reason. If they affirm it, then we ask: is the entailer (the whiteness) the same as the entailed (the non-existence of blackness) or different? If they say the same, that is contradictory because a thing cannot entail itself. If they say different, is that different thing intelligible (ma‘qūl)? If they say no, then how can they claim it is entailed? Judgment of entailment implies intelligibility. If they say yes, then that intelligible thing—the non-existence of blackness—is it eternal (qadīm) or originated (ḥādith)? If they say eternal, that is impossible because non-existence is not eternal. If they say originated, then how can something described by origination not be intelligible? If they say neither, that is impossible because before the occurrence of whiteness, the statement "blackness is non-existent" is false, and after it, it is true. Therefore, non-existence undoubtedly occurs. Since this occurrent is intelligible, it can be attributed to the power of a capable agent (qādir).

The second objection (al-wajh al-thānī) points out that some accidents have no opposite. For example, motion (ḥaraka) has no opposite; the opposition between motion and rest (sukūn) is, according to the philosophers, the opposition of disposition and privation (malaka wa ‘adam), i.e., existence and non-existence. Rest is simply the non-existence of motion. When motion ceases, no positive opposite occurs; it is pure non-existence. Similarly, qualities of perfection like the impression of sensible images in the eye or intelligible forms in the soul involve acquisition of existence without removal of an opposite. When they cease, it is the removal of existence without being followed by an opposite. Thus, their removal is pure non-existence that has occurred. Since the occurrence of non-existence is intellectually conceivable, it can be attributed to divine power.

The conclusion: it becomes clear that whenever an event occurs by an eternal will, it makes no difference whether what occurs is non-existence or existence. God knows best.

ضد
عدم
ملكة وعدم
سكون
حركة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Rest is an existent opposite of motion that occurs when motion ceases.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies that rest is merely the privation (non-existence) of motion, not a positive existent.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali's second objection, what is the nature of rest (sukūn) as understood by the philosophers?
A) Rest is an existent opposite of motion.
B) Rest is the pure non-existence of motion.
C) Rest is a substance that replaces motion.
D) Rest is an attribute eternal in God.

Key Takeaway
Some accidents cease without an opposite, proving that non-existence itself can be an occurrent effect of divine will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you lost a valuable ability, like strength, memory, or understanding. Did you attribute that loss to a physical cause (e.g., injury, aging) or to the direct will of Allah? How would Ghazali’s argument about non-existence being caused by God change your perspective on that event?
?
The lesson states that perfections like the impression of intelligible forms in the soul are granted and removed without an opposite. When you experience a moment of clarity or inspiration, do you recognize it as a direct gift from God, and when it fades, do you accept that its absence is also from Him? What would it mean for your humility and reliance on Allah to truly embrace this?
?
If non-existence itself is an event caused by God’s will, then every moment of loss in your life—whether of a loved one, a possession, or a capability—is not merely a natural accident but a direct act of divine creation. Can you look at the next loss you face and say, 'This too is from Allah, and it is a sign of His power and wisdom'? If not, what holds you back?

The Three Aspects of the Critique
Ghazali introduces three aspects that render the philosophers' claim of divine agency impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three fundamental reasons why philosophers cannot consistently claim that God is the agent of the world according to Ghazali.

Core Idea
God's agency requires will, novelty, and unity; philosophers deny all three.

Ghazali begins by exposing a contradiction in the philosophers' position. They claim God is the agent of the world, yet their own principles—denying divine attributes, asserting the world's eternity, and holding that only one proceeds from the One—make this claim untenable. In this lesson, you'll see the three aspects that Ghazali identifies as undermining their claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening passage
Read the Arabic passage from the beginning to where Ghazali introduces the three aspects. Notice his tone: he calls the philosophers' claim a 'deception' (talbīs) and says it is 'inconceivable' according to their own principles.
The Materialists (Dahriyya) are excluded from the philosophers mentioned.
2
Identify the three aspects
Ghazali lists three aspects: concerning the agent (fāʿil), concerning the act (fiʿl), and a common relation. As you read, summarize each in your own words.
The aspects are numbered in the text: وجه في الفاعل, وجه في الفعل, وجه في نسبة مشتركة.
3
Understand why each is a problem
For each aspect, ask: what do the philosophers believe that makes this aspect problematic? Write down the contradiction.
The commentary will help clarify the underlying philosophical positions.
4
Reflect on the structure
Notice how Ghazali sets up the discussion: he will verify each aspect and also consider the philosophers' counterarguments ('along with their imagination in repelling it').
This reveals Ghazali's thorough, dialectical method.

مسألة [3]
في بيان تلبيسهم بقولهم إن الله فاعل العالم وصانعه وأن العالم صنعه وفعله وبيان أن ذلك مجاز عندهم وليس بحقيقة
وقد اتفقت الفلاسفة سوى الدهرية على أن للعالم صانعاً، وأن الله تعالى هو صانع العالم وفاعله، وأن العالم فعله وصنعه، وهذا تلبيس على أصلهم؛ بل لا يتصور على مساق أصلهم أن يكون العالم من صنع الله، من ثلاثة أوجه:
وجه في الفاعل
ووجه في الفعل
ووجه في نسبة مشتركة بين الفعل والفاعل
أما الذي في الفاعل فهو أنه لابد أن يكون مريداً مختاراً عالماً بما يريده، حتى يكون فاعلا لما يريده، والله تعالى عندهم ليس مريداً، بل لا صفة له أصلا. وما يصدر عنه فيلزم منه لزوماً ضرورياً
والثاني أن العالم قديم والفعل هو الحادث
والثالث أن الله تعالى واحد عندهم من كل وجه، والواحد لا يصدر منه عندهم إلا واحد من كل وجه، والعالم مركب من مختلفات، فكيف يصدر عنه؟!
ولنحقق وجه كل واحد من هذه الوجوه الثلاثة، مع خيالهم في دفعه
أما الأول
فنقول: الفاعل عبارة عمن يصدر عنه الفعل مع الإرادة للفعل على سبيل الاختيار، ومع العلم بالمراد، وعندكم أن العالم من الله تعالى كالمعلول من العلة، يلزم لزوماً ضرورياً، لا يتصور من الله تعالى دفعه، كلزوم الظل من الشخص، والنور من الشمس وليس هذا من الفعل في شيء، بل من قال: إن السراج يفعل الضوء، والشخص يفعل الظل، فقد تجوز، وتوسع في التجوز توسعاً خارجاً عن الحد، واستعار اللفظ اكتفاء بوقوع المشاركة بين المستعار له والمستعار منه في وصف واحد، وهو أن الفاعل سبب على الجملة، والسراج سبب الضوء، والشمس سبب النور، ولكن الفاعل لم يسم فاعلا صانعاً، لمجرد كونه سبباً، بل لكونه سبباً على وجه مخصوص، وهو وقوع الفعل منه على وجه الإرادة والاختيار، حتى لو قال قائل: الجدار ليس بفاعل، والحجر ليس بفاعل، والجماد ليس بفاعل، وإنما الفعل للحيوان، لم ينكر عليه في ذلك، ولم يكن قوله كاذباً، وللحجر فعل عندهم، وهو الهوى والثقل والميل إلى المركز، كما أن للنار فعلا وهو التسخين، وللحائط فعلا، وهو الميل إلى المركز ووقوع الظل، فإن كل ذلك صادر منه وهو محال
فإن قيل: كل موجود ليس واجب الوجود بذاته وإنما هو موجود بغيره، فإنا نسمى ذلك الشيء مفعولا، ونسمى سببه فاعلا ولا نبالي كان السبب فاعلا بالطبع، أم بالإرادة، كما أنكم لا تبالون أنه كان فاعلاً بآلة أم بغير آلة، بل الفعل جنس، وينقسم إلى ما يقع بآلة، وإلى ما يقع بغير آلة، فكذلك هو جنس، وينقسم إلى ما يقع بالطبع وإلى ما يقع بالاختيار، بدليل أنا إذا قلنا فعل بالطبع، لم يكن قولنا بالطبع ضدا لقولنا فعل ولا دفعاً ونقضاً له، بل كان بياناً لنوع الفعل، كما أنا إذا قلنا فعل مباشرة من غير آلة، لم يكن نقضاً، بل كان تنويعاً وبياناً، وإذا قلنا فعل بالاختيار، لم يكن
Issue [3]: Explaining their deception in their saying that God is the agent (fāʿil) and maker (ṣāniʿ) of the world, and that the world is His making and act, and explaining that this is a metaphor (majāz) for them and not a reality. The philosophers, except for the Materialists (Dahriyya), have agreed that the world has a maker, and that God Most High is the maker and agent of the world, and that the world is His act and making. This is deception according to their own principle; rather, it is inconceivable according to the course of their principle that the world be from God's making, from three aspects: an aspect concerning the agent, an aspect concerning the act, and an aspect concerning a common relation between the act and the agent. As for that which concerns the agent, it is that He must be willing (murīd), choosing (mukhtār), knowing (ʿālim) what He wills, so that He may be an agent of what He wills. But God Most High, according to them, is not willing; rather, He has no attribute at all. What proceeds from Him follows necessarily (luzūman ḍarūriyyan). Second, that the world is eternal (qadīm) and the act is originated (ḥādith). Third, that God Most High, according to them, is one from every aspect, and from the one only one proceeds from every aspect, while the world is composed of diverse things; so how can it proceed from Him? Let us verify each of these three aspects, along with their imagination in repelling it. As for the first: We say: The agent (al-fāʿil) is an expression for one from whom the act proceeds with will (irāda) for the act by way of choice (ikhtiyār), and with knowledge (ʿilm) of the intended. According to you, the world from God Most High is like the effect (maʿlūl) from the cause (ʿilla), following necessarily, not conceivable for God Most High to repel it, like the following of the shadow from the person and the light from the sun. This is not part of 'act' at all. Rather, whoever says that the lamp makes (yafʿal) light, and the person makes (yafʿal) shadow, has used metaphor (tajawwaza) and extended in metaphor an extension beyond the limit, and borrowed the expression satisfied with the occurrence of sharing between the borrowed-for and borrowed-from in one description, which is that the agent is a cause (sabab) in general, and the lamp is a cause of light, and the sun is a cause of light. But the agent is not called an agent or maker merely because it is a cause, but because it is a cause in a specific manner, which is the occurrence of the act from it by way of will and choice. So much so that if someone said: 'The wall is not an agent, the stone is not an agent, the inanimate is not an agent; only animals perform acts,' he would not be denied in that, nor would his statement be false. According to them, the stone has an act, namely falling (hawā), weight (thiqal), and inclination (mayl) toward the center, just as fire has an act, namely heating (taskhīn), and the wall has an act, namely inclination toward the center and the occurrence of shadow. For all that proceeds from it is impossible (muḥāl). If it is said: Every existent that is not necessary of existence by itself but is existent by another, we call that thing an effect (mafʿūl) and we call its cause an agent (fāʿil), and we do not care whether the cause is an agent by nature (bi-l-ṭabʿ) or by will (bi-l-irāda), just as you do not care whether it is an agent with an instrument or without an instrument. Rather, 'act' (fiʿl) is a genus (jins), and it divides into what occurs with an instrument and what occurs without an instrument. Similarly, it is a genus and divides into what occurs by nature and what occurs by choice (ikhtiyār). The evidence is that if we say 'act by nature,' our saying 'by nature' is not contrary to our saying 'act' nor a repudiation or contradiction of it, but rather it is an explanation of the type of act. Just as if we say 'act directly without an instrument,' it is not a contradiction, but rather a division and explanation. And if we say 'act by choice,' it is not
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' claim that God is the agent of the world. Ghazali begins by stating the problem: the philosophers, except the Materialists (Dahriyya), agree that God is the maker and agent of the world, but this is a deception because according to their own principles, it cannot be true. He then lists three aspects that make their claim inconsistent: (1) regarding the agent: a true agent must be willing, choosing, and knowing, but the philosophers deny God has will or any attribute; (2) regarding the act: the philosophers say the world is eternal, but an act must be originated; (3) regarding the relation: from one only one can proceed, but the world is composed of diverse things. Ghazali then declares he will verify each aspect and begins with the first. He defines 'agent' (al-fāʿil) as one from whom the act proceeds with will and choice and with knowledge of the intended. He contrasts this with the philosophers' view that the world proceeds from God necessarily, like an effect from a cause, which is not agency. He gives examples: saying a lamp makes light or a person makes shadow is metaphorical, because the lamp and person are only causes in a general sense, but the term 'agent' is properly used only when the cause acts with will and choice. He reinforces this by noting that we do not call inanimate objects agents. He then anticipates an objection: the opponent might say that any cause of an effect can be called an agent, whether natural or voluntary, just as we call both direct and instrumental acts 'acts'. Ghazali replies that 'act' is a genus that can be divided into types, but calling natural causation 'act' does not negate the division; rather, it specifies the type. The objection and answer show Ghazali's linguistic and conceptual precision: while 'act' may be used broadly, the specific term 'agent' (fāʿil) in the full sense requires will and choice.

Key Terms
الفاعل (al-fāʿil)
الفعل (al-fiʿl)
المجاز (al-majāz)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume that since philosophers say God is the agent, they mean it in the same sense as Islamic theology.
Correction: Ghazali shows that according to their own principles, their claim is metaphorical and cannot be taken literally.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, which of the following is NOT one of the three aspects that make the philosophers' claim of divine agency impossible?
A) The agent must have will and choice
B) The act must be originated, but the world is eternal
C) The world's complexity cannot proceed from the One
D) The philosophers deny God's existence

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' claim that God is the agent of the world is internally inconsistent because they deny divine will, hold the world eternal, and maintain that only one proceeds from the One.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You affirm that God is the agent of all things. But do you truly live as though His will is the only cause that matters, or do you secretly attribute power to natural causes as the philosophers do? This lesson shows that true agency requires personal will and choice. When you receive a blessing, do you feel it came from a personal God who chose to give it to you, or just from a system? Which view shapes your gratitude?
?
If you deny God has attributes like will and knowledge — even in your actions — then what kind of maker are you worshipping? Examine your heart: are you tempted by the philosophers' view that God acts by necessity, like a machine? That would make Him no different from impersonal forces. Do you ever treat God as a 'force' rather than a personal being?
?
You read that from the One only one proceeds, yet the world is diverse. Do you accept the diversity in your life as from God, or do you assume some parts are from other causes? This lesson forces you to see that if God is truly the agent, then every single thing — your trials, your joys, your sins — must be within His will. Can you accept that, or do you reserve some aspect of your life as outside His control?

Causality and Created Things

True Agency Requires Will and Choice
Ghazali defines true agency as voluntary, contrasts it with necessary emanation, and addresses an objection about natural causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's definition of a true agent and his critique of the philosophers' notion of necessary emanation as a form of agency.

Core Idea
A true agent acts with will and choice, not by necessity.

In this lesson, Ghazali elaborates the first aspect: the concept of the agent itself. He defines agency as voluntary action with knowledge, and contrasts this with the philosophers' model of necessary emanation, which reduces God to a mere cause. He also addresses the objection that natural causes can be called agents, using examples like the lamp and the sun.

Guided Path
1
Read Ghazali's definition of an agent
Read the first part of the passage where Ghazali defines al-fāʿil. Note the three conditions: the act proceeds with will (irāda), choice (ikhtiyār), and knowledge (ʿilm).
Contrast this with the philosophers' view that God acts without will.
2
Understand the critique of necessary emanation
Ghazali says that according to the philosophers, the world proceeds from God necessarily, like a shadow from a person. He argues this is not agency at all, but a misuse of the term.
Notice the strong language: 'tajawwaza' (used metaphorically) and 'khārij ʿan al-ḥadd' (beyond the limit).
3
Examine the examples and the objection
Ghazali gives examples of inanimate objects that have effects (stone falling, fire heating) but are not called agents. Then he presents an objection: every cause can be called an agent, whether natural or voluntary.
The objection is introduced by 'fa-in qīla' (if it is said).
4
Analyze Ghazali's response
Ghazali responds that 'act' is a genus that can be divided into natural and voluntary, but calling natural causation 'act' does not make the cause a true agent. Agency requires a specific manner: will and choice.
The response is cut off in the text; try to infer the conclusion.

Third, that God Most High, according to them, is one from every aspect, and from the one only one proceeds from every aspect, while the world is composed of diverse things; so how can it proceed from Him? Let us verify each of these three aspects, along with their imagination in repelling it. As for the first: We say: The agent (al-fāʿil) is an expression for one from whom the act proceeds with will (irāda) for the act by way of choice (ikhtiyār), and with knowledge (ʿilm) of the intended. According to you, the world from God Most High is like the effect (maʿlūl) from the cause (ʿilla), following necessarily, not conceivable for God Most High to repel it, like the following of the shadow from the person and the light from the sun. This is not part of 'act' at all. Rather, whoever says that the lamp makes (yafʿal) light, and the person makes (yafʿal) shadow, has used metaphor (tajawwaza) and extended in metaphor an extension beyond the limit, and borrowed the expression satisfied with the occurrence of sharing between the borrowed-for and borrowed-from in one description, which is that the agent is a cause (sabab) in general, and the lamp is a cause of light, and the sun is a cause of light. But the agent is not called an agent or maker merely because it is a cause, but because it is a cause in a specific manner, which is the occurrence of the act from it by way of will and choice. So much so that if someone said: 'The wall is not an agent, the stone is not an agent, the inanimate is not an agent; only animals perform acts,' he would not be denied in that, nor would his statement be false. According to them, the stone has an act, namely falling (hawā), weight (thiqal), and inclination (mayl) toward the center, just as fire has an act, namely heating (taskhīn), and the wall has an act, namely inclination toward the center and the occurrence of shadow. For all that proceeds from it is impossible (muḥāl). If it is said: Every existent that is not necessary of existence by itself but is existent by another, we call that thing an effect (mafʿūl) and we call its cause an agent (fāʿil), and we do not care whether the cause is an agent by nature (bi-l-ṭabʿ) or by will (bi-l-irāda), just as you do not care whether it is an agent with an instrument or without an instrument. Rather, 'act' (fiʿl) is a genus (jins), and it divides into what occurs with an instrument and what occurs without an instrument. Similarly, it is a genus and divides into what occurs by nature and what occurs by choice (ikhtiyār). The evidence is that if we say 'act by nature,' our saying 'by nature' is not contrary to our saying 'act' nor a repudiation or contradiction of it, but rather it is an explanation of the type of act. Just as if we say 'act directly without an instrument,' it is not a contradiction, but rather a division and explanation. And if we say 'act by choice,' it is not
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' claim that God is the agent of the world. Ghazali begins by stating the problem: the philosophers, except the Materialists (Dahriyya), agree that God is the maker and agent of the world, but this is a deception because according to their own principles, it cannot be true. He then lists three aspects that make their claim inconsistent: (1) regarding the agent: a true agent must be willing, choosing, and knowing, but the philosophers deny God has will or any attribute; (2) regarding the act: the philosophers say the world is eternal, but an act must be originated; (3) regarding the relation: from one only one can proceed, but the world is composed of diverse things. Ghazali then declares he will verify each aspect and begins with the first. He defines 'agent' (al-fāʿil) as one from whom the act proceeds with will and choice and with knowledge of the intended. He contrasts this with the philosophers' view that the world proceeds from God necessarily, like an effect from a cause, which is not agency. He gives examples: saying a lamp makes light or a person makes shadow is metaphorical, because the lamp and person are only causes in a general sense, but the term 'agent' is properly used only when the cause acts with will and choice. He reinforces this by noting that we do not call inanimate objects agents. He then anticipates an objection: the opponent might say that any cause of an effect can be called an agent, whether natural or voluntary, just as we call both direct and instrumental acts 'acts'. Ghazali replies that 'act' is a genus that can be divided into types, but calling natural causation 'act' does not negate the division; rather, it specifies the type. The objection and answer show Ghazali's linguistic and conceptual precision: while 'act' may be used broadly, the specific term 'agent' (fāʿil) in the full sense requires will and choice.

السبب (al-sabab)
المجاز (al-majāz)
العلة (al-ʿilla)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any cause of an effect qualifies as an 'agent' in the same way.
Correction: Ghazali shows that true agency (fāʿiliyya) requires the cause to act with will and choice; natural causes are only causes in a general sense.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what distinguishes a true agent from a mere cause?
A) The agent produces its effect necessarily
B) The agent acts with will and choice
C) The agent is a living being
D) The agent acts directly without instruments

Key Takeaway
True agency is defined by will and choice; without them, causation is merely metaphorical and does not constitute genuine agency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily actions, how often do you act with full will, choice, and knowledge versus simply reacting out of habit or external pressure? What would it take for you to become a more conscious agent in your own life?
?
This lesson teaches that true agency requires will and choice. When you worship Allah, do you do so as a mindful, willful servant, or as a routine act like a machine? What steps can you take today to ensure your worship is a genuinely voluntary act of love and submission?
?
If God's creation is not a necessary emanation but an act of His will, how does this change the way you relate to Him? Are you grateful that He chose to create you and sustain you, or do you take it for granted as if it were necessary?
